I Dr. M. PERRTT,
M.R.U.S., L.S.A , &c.,
SPECIALIST for the Treatment of Nervous
Complaints, Diabetes, Tumors, &c.
Registered by the Medical Bo ird of N.S. W. Late Home Sur-
geon t > the Great Northern Hospital , London.
I am aware that I am not strictly following: the paths
Of professional etiquette by advertising myself as a
Specialist for the tieatment of this class of disease, but,
unfortunately for the suffering public in Australia, the
apathy shown by Qualified Mon in the treatment of
Chronic and Norton* Complaints has induced men of the
lowest grade of intellect (whose only qualifications are
unlimited assurance) to spring up, and by specious
advertisements and false promises trap the unwary.
It is very evident that sufferers from these diseases
have been scandalously treated by these unqualified
persons The evidence taken at the Medical Enquiry
held in Sydney (published in the Daily Telegraph , Nov.
6th. 7th and Bth, 1890) plainly shows the disgraceful
manner in which patients are duped. Not only are
they robbed of their money, but what is of infinitely
greater importance, their health is injured also. It is
therefore time that Legally Qualified .lien entered the
field to fight these peats ®f society with their own
weapons, viz., Advertising.
It is utterly impossible for those who are suffering
from thc.-e disease*, who are scattered over nil parts of
Australasia, to ascertain which Qualified Practitioner
makes these diseases his speciality unless he advertises.
I consider, therefore, that by advertising I not only
strike a blow at the Quackery which now exists, but I also add
to the usefulness of my profession and in no way lessen my
personal dignity.
My Qualifications I Invite the fullest Investigation Into. For some considerable time I was House Surgeon to the
Great Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of observing the many phases of the Nervous and
Sexual System, and to become acquainted with the most scientific methods of ellectlng their cure. , , ,
My sole attention is given to the treatment of these diseases, and when a person consults me he is Informed at the
commencement whether I can effect his cure or not. The patient is not deluded with false and specious promises, and
If I undertake the case
S Absolutely Guarantee a Complete and Permanent Cure.
My great success in the treatment of these diseases enables me to give tins guarantee without fear.

instances is sufficient to check the progress of disease. This Book will be forwarded Free on application.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief
Surgeon of the long-established and favourably-

ceived his medical education in the leading colleges
and hospitals of Europe. He has devoted a life-
time to the study of
PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIM
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest
and safest methods by which they may be eradi-
cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to his
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in
the water, accompanied by slight burning or
smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during
the day, circles round the eyes. Giddiness,
irregularity of the bowels, specks before the eyes,
bashfulness, discontentedness, weakness or pains
in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,
buzzing noises in the ears and head, pimples, a
fear that something dreadful is about to happen,
weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking
cough, who cannot marry, nervousness in society,
wakefulness, palpitation of the heart, genera!
weakness, and other symptoms which lead to in-
sanity and death, should consult him, for he has
mastered these difficulties. On receipt of fid. in

however, that all plurals of such words may ha given a
separate words. Now oral must he used more than once
Tire fact that any word might he made more t an once bj
rising duplicate letters contained in the competitive won
shall not affect this last point. No word will he allowec
that contains any letter oftener than the same occurs ii
the competitive word. Competitors should arrange tlioi
words alphabetically. No member of the Committee wil
be allowed to compete. All lists must be sent in, addressee

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 7, 1891.
“Move On!”
It is a somewhat ominous coincidence that
Parson Langley, of Sydney, and Parson
Booth, of the, world at large, should have
simultaneously struck the same remedy for
the same disease, for the remedy is likely to
be a good deal overdone in consequence.
The pastor in the blood-and-lire shirt pro-
poses to cure the chronic distress of Britain
by exporting a select gathering of British
paupers to work on farms in Australia, and
almost at the same moment the pastor in the
sombre tail-coat proposes to cure the chronic
distress of Sydney by shifting another
gathering of local “ unemployed ” to work
on farms somewhere in the interior. In each
case it is only a new variety of the same old
nostrum ; the philanthropist never, by any
chance* attempts to find and remove the
causes of poverty—his one invariable pre-
scription is that the poor must be “ sent ”
Somewhere in the hope that they may at last
strike a Paradise where work is plentiful,
and wages abundant, and where the labour-
market is not overstocked, and there is no
Vvant or suffering of any description. It is
true that the unemployed difficulty is raging
everywhere* from Australia to Iceland, “and
from Chili to Labrador, and in every country
there are too many labourers and too little
employment, and the policy of emigration
has degenerated into an aimless swapping of
inhabitants t hat leads to no visiblegood results.
It is impossible to suppose or to believe that
the two parsons are not fully aware of these
facts, but the force of habit was too strong for
them. Some day, possibly, their theory will
be worked out to its logical conclusion, and
then the world at large will go in for the
systematic export of all its other diseases as
well as the disease of hunger, and the Aus-
tralian with typhoid will be exported to
Brazil, and the Brazilian with yellow fever
will be shunted off to Australia, and the man
with a broken leg and a damaged spine will
be sent to Japan instead of to the hospital,
and the person who has been run over by a
cab will be despatched to Northern Queens-
land, and there will be a general exchange of
incurable tumours and abscesses and similar
properties. At present, however, this policy
is only in the earliest stages of development.
The individual who is broken up by hunger
and want of employment is sent into the
country, or into some other country, where
things are a shade worse than they were in
his original home, and the person who is
broken up through a wall falling on his head
is treated on the spot, whereas a more
reasonable and philanthropic course would
be to run him into the next State by
means of a barrow or an ambulance, and
leave him there.
* * •
According to Pastor Langley’s scheme of
benevolence the unemployed of Sydney are
to go out into the wilderness and devote
themselves to agriculture, and according to
the scheme of Pastor Booth the unemployed
of London are to do likewise. In each case
it is assumed that when the poor take to
growing food they will be placed, at least,
beyond the reach of starvation, and thus, in
theory at least, all the distress on earth can
be alleviated without any alteration of exist-
ing social conditions, by the simple expedi-
ent of sending every hungry citizen out into
a waste place, where he may grow carrots
and other wholesome refreshment and be at
rest. But in practice this project breaks
down miserably. If land was absolutely
free, and food was the one essential —if the
agriculturist could go hatless and unshod and
naked, and if he never wanted to have his
hair cut—if there was neither rent nor taxes,
nor any other liabilities whatever—then the
plan of exporting the poor into the wilder-
ness as a cure for poverty might produce
admirable results. But, unhappily, the
agriculturist finds money an absolute essen-
tial, and it can only be obtained by the sale
of his produce, and as the world can only
buy and pay for a certain fixed and limited
quantity of agricultural produce at the best,
anything in excess of that limit is absolutely
valueless. The benevolent scheme which the
Reverend Booth proposes to adopt on a large
scale in England, and which the Reverend
Langley proposes to follow on a small scale
in New South Wales is practically founded on
the assumption that all the superfluous por-
tion of the human race can resort to agri-
cultural pursuits provided the opportunity is
allowed them, but in reality there is no more
an unlimited demand for agriculturists than
there is for mesmerists, or prize-fighters, or
policemen. It is as reasonable to propose
that all the unemployed of Sydney should at
once take refuge in the Church or the Law
as that they should take refuge at the plough
or the harrow ; the demand for carrots and
the demand for poetry are equally incapable
of expansion beyond a certain point.
* # #
The world already produces almost as much
food as it can reasonably consume, and even
if the whole human race enjoyed three
square meals every day, it is questionable if
there would be any deficiency.* Famines, it
is true, are plentiful enough, but they arise
solely through want of money to buy pro-
visions—the provisions themselves are there
in abundance. Even in the awful years of
hunger in Ireland some two genera-
tions ago, when a hundred thousand
human beings perished from absolute want,
Ireland, fed by American war-ships, actually
exported vast quantities of grain and dairy
produce to England and elsewhere. The
failure of the potato-crop loft half the Irish
population penniless, but still they starved
in the midst of abundance, and died only
because they had no means wherewith to
purchase even the smallest portion of the
plentiful supplies that were leaving the
country by every seaport in order to pay the
demands of the grasping absentee landlord.
Similarly in the year of the great Indian
famine, India was still one of the principal
food-exporting countries of the globe, but
the Hindoos died off in incalculable numbers
because the landowner and the tax-gatherer
had to be satisfied first, and after these two
British cormorants were filled there was
nothing left. In no civilised country upon
the earth does poverty arise through want of
food, and the idea of abolishing poverty by
employing the hunger-stricken masses on the
land is as hopelessly impracticable as the
idea of reaching the same end by employing
them in making boilers. If the world’s food
supply is doubled by creating an artificial
boom in agriculture, the poor will be as poor
and as hungry as they are to-day ; and if the
supply of boilers is doubled the results will
be just the same.
* * *
But despite these obvious facts it has
become the fixed habit of modern philan-
thropists to assume that poverty and all
kinds of human trouble arise solely from the
inability of the sufferers to reach that un-
specified region where work is plentiful and
wages abundant, and where every toiler sits
down beneath his own vine and wattle-tree,
and is at rest. Nobody ever seems to live
or to have lived in this region, and nobody
knows definitely where it is : but everybody
agrees that it is not where he is himself but
always where somebody else is, and conse-
quently the one great anxiety of the Chris-
tian is to send his unfortunate brother there
—wherever it may be—and let him find his
pathway strewn with flowers and things
generally, overflowing with milk and honey.
Therefore the kind-hearted inhabitant of the
plains lends his assistance to shunt his
suffering friend into the city, and the kind-
hearted inhabitant of the city becomes filled
with sympathy and shunts his suffering
friend out on to the plains ; and the
humane cornstalk citizen sends the
poor on to Melbourne that they may be
tilled with happiness and bliss, and the soul-
ful preacher of Adelaide gives them another
lift in the name of the Loard as far as
Perth, and everywhere the results are the
same. The land of poverty is everywhere
and the land of plenty is nowhere ; and
every industry from the Law and the
Church down to the humble stone-breaking
pursuit, is equally overstocked. And yet
when a new philanthropic movement is
initiated the object is always to send the
“ unemployed ” somewhere else, while the
real evil lies in the fact that everywhere two
men are endeavouring to do the work of
three because they have to maintain the
landlord and the capitalist and the middle-
man as well as themselves, and as the
amount of profitable employment is strictly
limited the other man must necessarily be
left out in the cold.
The “Supreme” Court v. the Privy
Council.
Says the Argus, dealing with the Messer v.
Gibbs titles ease, in which the Victorian
Registrar of Titles successfully appealed to the
Privy Council against the Victorian Full
Court
“ It is undoubtedly somewhat of a shock to local lesral
prejudices, to find the considered judgment of Mr.
Justice Webb— probably the ablest of our equity lawyers
—confirmed as it was by a Full Court, consisting of'Mr.
Justice A'Beckett— another skilled equity judge—the
Chief Justice, and the late Mr. Justice Kerfbrd, upset by
the Privy Council, seeing that the act to be construed
was of colonial origin, and the practice surroundin'' the
case a purely local creation.”
Quite so, but the Argus will soon recover.
The shock is not of sufficient voltage to have
any deadly or lasting influence on the Melbourne
heavy-weight. Instead of going into the merits
of the case, as the Argus does, the proper
question to ask oneself in this case is, whether
appeals should be made by our Governments,
under any circumstances, to what is practically
a foreign Court. The judges of the Privy
Council go into a case of this kind absolutely
ignorant of colonial statute law, and, as the
An Toy case shows,. quite ignorant of our
constitutional rights. This appealing to the
British lawyers is a growing habit in Victoria.
Miss Starr’s case was decided against the
Victorian Government, and the equity of the
decision of our Court was at any time as plain
as some of the members of the Cabinet of that
day. The Gibbs case, above noted, came next,
and now the Chinese case is sub judice.
# # #
Apart from the shocks at the result of a
specially-imported verdict there is another and
a greater shock which has been overlooked by
the Argus. That is the shock caused by the
expression of want of confidence by the State in
its own Supreme Court, which is shown by the
act of appealing from its decision. Supreme
did we say ?—what court is there in Australia
that is supreme, except, perhaps, in a Hiber-
nian sense? Just look at an American example.
Their Supreme Court has powers higher than
those of any Legislature in the land. It can set
the President and Congress at defiance, and
from its decision there is no appeal. Law is
supreme in America. But in Victoria, Higin-
botham and Webb and A’Beckett, men whose
character and knowledge of the law which they
administer are unquestioned, are practically
told by the Government of the land in which
they form a Supreme Court that if their jiidg-.
ments are not subservient to the policy of the
day strangers will be called in to reverse it !
* * #
Arguments on the merits of the cases ob-
scures the great principle that the Australian
Governments should abide by the decision of
their respective highest Courts, even at the risk
of hardship. If that decision be against their
contention or their ideas of policy or injustice,
it is open to them to pass an Act to prevent
the recurrence of a similar case. It would be
unpleasant, for instance, to admit that we
could not prevent an influx of Chinese, but if a
majority of our Supreme Court say that under
our present laws we cannot, it is much more
dignified and conformable to national feeling
to alter the law than to appeal to the Privy
Council. It would, perhaps, be futile to legis-
late so as to prevent such appeals, for while we
are connected with Britain the Crown could
hardly be expected to assent to an Act that
would deny the supremacy of Imperial Courts
in any part of the Empire, but it should be
asserted with no uncertain sound that Govern-
ment appeals to the Privy Council would be
vastly more honoured in the breach than the
observance.
Imperial Federation Again.
The question of the future of Australia has at
last been satisfactorily and definitely settled.
It may now be neatly tied up in a piece of
bright red tape and put away in a pigeonhole,
to rest undisturbed till the day of the last
trump. Lord Carington has put the case in a
nutshell. Australia never wants to separate
from dear old England, not she. Australians
don’t want any voice in the management of the
Empire, not they. They are perfectly happy
and easy in their minds so long as the House of
Lords is there to look after them. Why should
they want to fuddle their brains about the
steering of the ship of state, and all that
nonsense ? The British lion is at the helm and
the white ensign is at the peak. Hip, hip, hip,
hooray !
The colonists knew that England would regard the
invasion of Australia in the same light as they would the
landing of an enemy in Kent. England would aid Aus-
tralia against a foreign foe, even if Canada objected: and,
on the other hand, England would not wait the consent
of Australia before sending, if required, cruisers flying
the white flag to the Bay of Fundy or. to the Behring Sea.
* * #
That is the sort of stuff they cheer at the
Colonial Institute. It is adjusted with mathe-
matical precision to the intellectual calibre of
an assemblage whose character is so innocently,
but conclusively, sized in the Sydney Morning
Herald’s cabled enumeration of the most dis-
tinguished notabilities present—The Prince of
Wales, the Earl of Granville, the Duke of
Fife, Lord Knutsford, and Mr. James R.
Fairfax. (We can’t restrain here a paren-
thetical burst of brotherly journalistic pride
in the modest shrinking spirit of self-
effacement with which the S.M.H. introduced
three unimportant names between those of the
Prince of Wales and “Mr. James.”) It is
another proof of Lord Carington’s power of
taking the measure of his audience that he did
not feel called upon to give them anything new.
The Bay of Fundy outburst did duty as part of
his last speech at the Sydney University nearly
12 months ago, and was received with so much
loyal fervour by Sir William Manning, Gover-
nor Hopetoun, and other representative Aus-
tralians who had seats on the dais, that he
evidently thought it good enough to put by in a
cool place to keep till it could be dished up
again for English use. And, to tell the truth,
it is not at all bad—for a lord. It sums up con -
cisely and comprehensively pretty well all that
can be said in favour of the fashionable English
idea of Australian loyalty. “Be a good little
boy and cheer the dear, good Queen, and we’ll
see that the naughty Russian doesn’t bite you.”
Unfortunately the little boy is getting sense
enough to see that if ho didn’t cheer the Queen
the naughty Russian wouldn’t want to bite
him.
* # . #
The Australian mind has been clearing itself
rapidly of late years, and one thing it has
very definitely made itself up about is that
Australia is not going to long continue
the English connection on the present
lop-sided basis. We do not propose to
spend money on forts and ironclads to light the
enemies of England, without knowing what the
light is about. If England gets into a quarrel
over her own private concerns, we have to
suffer for it; and as it is no business of ours,
England ought to stand the racket. And we
don’t propose to go in for any slobbering
gratitude about it either. Even if England
had no trade out hero to protect, she has no
right to let Australia be damaged in an affair
which Australia had nothing to do with. If
Lord Salisbury- wants to fool along into a war
with the United States over a boatload of cod-
fish, let the people whose ballots put him into
power pay the piper. Who made him a ruler
over us ? If we are to shoulder our muskets,
it must be at the bidding of our own chosen
leaders, and not of somebody who governs an
island on the other side of the world, and who
only happens to govern it because five years
ago some hundreds of thousands of voters
shared his views on the propriety of propa-
gating loyalty in Ireland with the point of the
bayonet. If the British Government is to
plunge us into war, we must have access to the
ballot-box that creates the British Government.
That, in short, is the case for Imperial
Federation.
* * *
And a very good case it is, ex-Governor Car-
ington to the contrary, notwithstanding. It
would be an unanswerable case if the only
alternative were a continuance of government
from Downing-street. But it is not the only
alternative. The present system is an impos-
sible one, and shows signs of an impending
break-down in about 13 different places, but an
Imperial Federation wouldn’t carry things
much farther, even if it could be set going.
The Australian party in the federal senate
would be the antitype of the Irish party in the
House of Commons—only getting attention to
its most vital concerns by the persistent ob-
struction of all other business, and then being
ruthlessly clotured into silence. That little
difficulty certainly wouldn’t take long to settle,
but we don’t see the need of having to settle it
at all. England need not worry herself over
any prospective “ Australian question.” When
the Australian question arises it will be dealt
with by the independent Australian nation.
That is why Australians don’t get up any
enthusiasm about Imperial Federation. Im-
perial Federation would only be the half-way
house on a roundabout road to the Republic,
and Australians are not in the habit of going-
round when anything is to be saved by taking
the short cut.

Two Women and a Do g.
This world is all a stage, and the Devil is the
scene-shifter, and Nemesis is the property-man,
and the Furies take the money at the door and
look after things generally. Here are two acts
from the show which we quote in justification
of this view
Colonel North, the nitrate
millionaire, who recently
paid £BSO for a single dog,
keeps a large number at
Eltham, where the kennels
are sumptuously fitted up.
There is a doctor’s room,
where a dog who has a
slight cold or other ailment
is immediately attended to.
There is a cloak-room ; the
dogs’ coats cost about 10s.
each. In the feeding-room
you see shoulders of mutton
and prime pieces of beef
weighing about 281 b. each.
Another glimpse ol East
End life (and death) was
afforded by an inquest held
by Coroner E. Baxter on
the body of Elizabeth An-
stead, 34, a single woman,
lately-residing at 13 ('aider
street, Bromley. Mary Ann
Anstead, an old woman
scarcely able to walk, stater
that deceased was hei
daughter. They lived to
gether, and deceased usee
to work at shirt-finishing,
at which she earned 3s. pei
week, and sometimes not
that. Witness was allowee
2s. 6d. per week as outdooi
relief, and" this representee
their total income. The>
paid 2s. per week rent foi
their room, and lived ant
clothed themselves out tv
the other 3s. 6d. The de
ceased lived chiefly on breat
and butter. On Monday
morning she was worse, ani
she died the same night
The Coroner: “ This sort o'
thing goes on all the wee!
round, yet people seem tc
think there is no poverty h
the East End.”
To be a hardworking English female is a pool
way of earning a living, but it is obviously a
grand institution to be an English dog. Yet il
Elizabeth Anstead had applied for a billet as
one of Colonel North’s dogs she would probably
have been rejected with derision because it was
her misfortune to be only a human being—the
glorious prospect of being an £BSO dog was
closed against her by the iron decrees of fate,
and she never had a show because she couldn’t
bark. Looking at the matter from an economic
standpoint, it would be interesting to know
what is the cause of human beings in England,
and whether any steps are being taken to eradi-
cate them. In a country where a woman and
her mother are only worth 3s. a week, while a
dog, altogether apart from its mother, is worth
£BSO, it is obviously unprofitable to grow
women at all. The dog, we presume, would
get on perfectly well without them—at all
events there are evidently more women than
any dog can possibly require ; for instance, no
dog or dog-owner seems to have had any visible
or conceivable necessity for the ancient Mary
Ann and her offspring, and under these circum-
stances it is difficult to imagine why they
existed at all. < Probably neither of the miser-
able females could have thrown any light on
the matter if the question had been put to


them, and it is quite certain that nobody ever
thought it worth while to put the question to
either of them. And now that one of them is
gone, no one seems any the poorer, and if the
other one would only go likewise the parish
would be two-and-sixpence a week better off,
and the only thing they have left behind them
is this arithmetical problem : If an English
woman is a dead loss of Is. 3d., and a dog is a
profit of £BSO, how many women, according to
the laws and customs of British Christianity,
would require to be killed in order to save
enough money to keep one fashionable pup
dive ? The Nation of Shopkeepers would do
well to look into this point. A race which is
wasting its time in raising unmarketable human
beings—for exportation to the Australian dust-
heap —when it might devote itself to the culti-
vation of a dividend-paying mongrel, has wil-
fully shut its eyes to the main chance, and has
forgotten the great axioms of Trade.
The Deceased War-Scare.
That old European war-scare which has seen so
much hard service in recent years seems to have
died a natural death, and been reverently put
away in the grave. It did much good work
while it lasted, but the venerable bogey got
worn-out in the end and crumbled away. For
nearly ten years the energetic cable-liar sent
along his unassuming paragraph about three
times a week, stating that the day of unmiti-
gated smash was at hand, and the car of Jug-
gernaut, or something equally imposing, was
about to burst out from its retirement and
squash the world into an unpleasant mass ; and
generally the world believed the statement and
waited anxiously for the yell of the political
volcano that was always going to explode to-
morrow. The trouble had a good many
different shapes. Sometimes the uncle of
somebody who was dead was hovering about
an inaccessible mountain in Asia, and at
other times there was a bold statement
to the effect that some hazy individual had
been seen in Bulgaria and “ serious complica-
tions were expected. ” Generally somebody was
gathering an army on a frontier somewhere,
and at other times somebody presented an
ultimatum which was never heard of after-
wards ; and, whatever else happened, it was
generally understood that the ‘ ‘ war-cloud ”
must burst at an early date because all Europe
was in arms and howling for the fray. Yet it
never seemed to dawn on anybody that when
France and Germany, or any other two
Powers, have two million soldiers each
they are no more likely to fight than if they
had only 40,000 —in fact, they are a good
deal less likely to do so, because though
men are plentiful money is lamentably scarce,
and the average European exchequer would
give out before the enemies could be brought
within gunshot of each other, and even the
preliminary skirmish would have to be fought
on tick. If the Great Powers maintained only
one soldier apiece, war would be practically
incessant on account of its cheapness; if they
had six millions, war would be about extinct,
for even an autumn manoeuvre would leave an
empire insolvent. The “bloated armaments”
which have grown up during the last 20 years
have kept Europe quiet, for they are too heavy
to be dragged about with impunity ; and there
was never any war-scare at all except in the
lying cablegram, and the “ war-cloud ” was an
old, worn-out myth, and the “prevailing un-
easiness,” and tbe “strained relations,” and
the rest of the literary material were mostly
infamous bosh. When the Whitechapel fiend
and the great London strike got into full blast,
the cable fiend forgot, in the pressure of busi-
ness, to keep the ancient wheeze going any
longer, and when he had no time to telegraph
about impending wars, the wars ceased to
impend right off. The great diplomatic com-
plication broke up because no one had leisure
to read about it, and probably many a genuine
war could be broken up in the same fashion, if
the parties chiefly concerned would go right
along with their business and take no notice of
it whatever.
Freedom of Contract.
There is now in type, and shortly to be issued,
a work that for some time past has been
urgently needed by the capitalist employers
and their organs, the capitalist press. Its full
title is to be “Anew and revised dictionary
of words and terms in common use relating to
the pretensions of the labouring classes.”
The object of the book is by supplying
a set of amended definitions to save sub-
sidised journalists the exhausting labour
of proving at great length whenever
any labour troubles occur that ordinary
English expressions mean what they want them
to mean. An unfortunate delay in the issue of
the work has put the daily press to great in-
convenience in dealing with the dispute between
Vicars and Cos., of Sydney, and their child-
employes A simple definition of this kind—-
“ Freedom of Contract—-the right of the em-
ployer to impose his own terms on his own ser-
vants ” —would have made the leader-writer’s
business easy. It is confoundedly hard lines on a
poor pressman to expect him to make out that
it is a violation of the principle of freedom of
contract for wage-earners to say that they don’t
choose to work for a given employer unless he
reinstates a fellow wage-earner who has been
in their opinion unjustly dismissed. This is
what the daily press calls “intolerable dicta-
tion.” Perhaps it is ; but that doesn’t make it
any interference with freedom of contract.
‘ Intolerable dictation,” by the way, is a term
with a pretty variable meaning. A thing is
intolerable when you can’t put up with it. That
depends on how much you can stand. For the last
40 or 50 centuries the labourer has stood the most
damnable oppression, simply because he has
had to stand it. To-day he is learning the
virtue of combination, and is beginning to tell
his employer that he won’t stand it. That is
what, in the employers’ jargon, is called “in-
tolerable dictation. It is intolerable so long
as the employer can't stand it. He will soon
learn in his turn that he has to stand it. When
he has taken that lesson properly to heart, it
will be a convenient time to commence putting
freedom of contract” on a fair basis. Meanwhile
and pending the contemplated revision of the
English language, freedom of contract means
the same for one side as the other. It means
that the employer may offer work on his own
terms, and refuse to employ anyone except on
those terms. It means, on the other hand, that
the wage-earner may propose what terms
he likes, even as to the men he chooses
to wmrk with, and refuse to accept
employment if his terms are not agreed
to. Freedom of contract, in short, means
that no contract need be entered into that is
not acceptable to both sides. And it means,
therefore, the right to either side to hold out
until the other side finds it more advantageous
to give in than to hold out any longer. That is
the philosophy of strikes. It will take a lot of
new dictionaries to convince the level-headed
workers of Australia that there is anything
inconsistent between a strike and the freedom
of contract that a strike is at present the only
means of enforcing.

The Departure of Capital.
Every time there occurs anywhere in Australia
a disagreement between Capital and Labour,
the Tory press raises a discordant howl to the
effect that Capital will presently be driven out
of the country, and Australia will go to ruin in
consequence. Asa rule, three-fourths of the
Australian nation becomes scared at this in-
telligence, under the influence of a vague,
shapeless impression that Capital has hitherto
done something for them, and that when it is
driven out it will cease to do that something,
and, consequently, everybody will be left.
There is something large and wild and ominous
about this oft-repeated threat, and though
nobody knows exactly how Capital comes to a
country, or what it does when it is there, or
how it leaves again, or what happens when it
does leave, and though nobody ever heard of
Capital leaving any country unless it had picked
the bones of that country absolutely bare, the
mere suggestion that something of an impres-
sive nature is about to happen has a certain
awe-inspiring influence upon the country in
general. The next time the old wheeze comes
to the front, however, it might be as well if
that section of the press which props up the
interests of wealth would explain itself a little
more fully. The Capital of Australia—as of
every other country—consists almost entirely
of buildings, land and mineral deposits,
and as these three things are not of a
portable nature, they, at all events, will
not be driven out at present. The fences
and the tanks, the waterworks, harbours,
roads, breakwaters, wharves, railways, tram-
ways, and similar objects will not rise up and
quit, and though cattle, sheep, and horses are
more or less removeable, they are nob suffi-
ciently so to start off en masse for England—
the country where Australia’s liabilities are
mostly situated, and to which its assets would
naturally depart when the foreign investor quits
Australia in disgust. With regard to the float-
ing capital of the country, it consists largely of
money invested on good “ shecurity ” and at a
high rate of interest, and if it should give way
to unreasoning panic at some future date and
take unexpectedly to flight, it must inevitably
leave the interest and the “ shecurity ” behind
—in other words, the capitalist may go, but the
capital must remain. When this substance
desires to make a dignified exit, the first essen-
tial is to realise on the investment by passing it
on to some other speculator, but in the general
retreat, which is so frequently foretold, and
which never comes to pass, it will be impossible
to sell to anybody, and consequently capital
cannot leave even if it tried. It might leave
just sufficiently to cut off a great part of the
supply of imports, and thereby it would
create a great boom in Australian indus-
tries ; and it might also make a general
realisation at a sacrifice of 90 per cent, or there-
abouts, and thus relieve Australia of a great
burden of debt at a very low figure, but beyond
this the effort to drive out foreign Capital
seems about as hopeless as the attempt to drive
out the mountains. Therefore, the next time
the horgans of wealth announce to the work-
men of this country that their conduct must
inevitably drive Capital out of their native land,
they might also explain the process by which it
goes out of any land, and how it looks when it
is going. There have been many cases in which
Capital was destroyed by war, neglect, mis-
government, or other causes, but the spectacle
of this solid substance solemnly packing up its
carpet-bag and departing with tears in its eyes
is one which the world has never yet witnessed.
At the present moment it is making a stupen-
dous effort to leave the territories of the Ar-
gentine Confederation, though it went there
quite recently, but to the great grief of the
British investor three hundred millions of it
has got stuck and won’t come out. And that’s
what is the matter with the Barings. *•
Glimpses of our Republic.
SIDE-bursts towards the full freedom of an Aus-
tralian Republic are constantly “ centrifnga-
lising. ” The latest prominent indications have
inhered in the question as to whether or not an
Australian colony is a sovereign state—that is
to say, whether a colonial Government can con-
stitutionally commit an act of sovereign power.
Asa matter of dry existent law, an Australian
colony is simply a British dependency, yet there
are Australian Premiers who demand and even
grasp certain powers that utterly ignore this
fact, while the Imperial Government is wise
enough to handle certain questions gingerly
and to shelve them if possible. In the Ah
Toy appeal case the Privy Council simply
shirked the great constitutional question in
which Victoria was interested. Had the
Privy Council declared that a colony could
perform a sovereign act, it would have
established a precedent and displeased China—
that “ conuug Power” which it is now recog-
nised British policy to conciliate. On the
other hand, had the Privy Council formally
ruled in precise and forbidding terms that a
Victorian Government could not perform a
sovereign act, it would have set the colonies
“ all on end,” though it placated China. From
a British point of view, the question was wisely
evaded. Premier Munro now desires to have
the Imperial recognition of his contention tha t
each colony should be able to execute commer-
cial treaties on its own account with Foreign
Powers. This is the same blister in another
place, and, naturally, John Bull would avoid
direct dealing with it. Diplomacy has infinite
resources. A late cablegram from London
reads: “ France refuses to recognise the right
of Victoria to terminate the Postal Convention
concerning the transit of Australian mail
matter through France, except through Britain ,”
&c. France is thus put up to answer the chal-
lenge to which Great Britain could not safely
respond.

Hopetoun’s Fudge.
Under the exciting influence of an A.N.A.
banquet, Gov. Hopetoun seems to have lost his
little head for a moment on the recent anni-
versary of Transportation Day. Plunging
wildly into the dangerous subject of “ loyalty,”
this pallid young gentleman remarked that
“Of course, now and then, people let a word
or two drop which was foolish, but he
did not think they meant very much. The
best proof that they lived under a free
system of Government was that they were
allowed to utter such sentiments without
receiving punishment. If in France or America
any fellow got up and made a speech in favour
of getting a king on the throne of France, he
would be seized there and then by a couple of
policemen and walked off to the nearest lock-
up. ” Verdant folly is its own punishment in
this world, and we may assume that Hopetoun,
when he found that even the obsequious handful
of Natives there present had no cheers to uree
him further along his benighted way, felt sorry
he had spoken. Anyway, he stammered, looked
pitiably uncomfortable, and sat down suddenly,
explaining that he wouldn’t occupy any more
time. Whereupon the Natives broke into a
ruminative “Hear, hear.” Australia expects
little of its Governors, nor wants that little
long, but those ornamental personages are at
least required to remain as unobjectionable as
possible, and refrain from slandering institutions
more enlightened than those they represent.
Without considering the sense or otherwise of
Hopetoun’s assertion as referring to France
—a country where a few howlingly
Royalist or Buonapartist newspapers are
still published and a few interested rogues
or gratuitous idiots still drink to the health of a
visionary king or emperor at public dinneis—
we will lake the callow lordling on the American
question. No Australian has ever been heard
to “let drop a word or two” in favour of a
Republic like that of France, which, though
the deliberate choice of the people, exists only
on the sufferance of a large standing army, and
is as yet a long way from being free in fact.
But America is a name ever on the tip of the
democratic tongue —the one constant reminder
that kings, courts, lords, perpetual pensions,
and the thousand and one vanities and infamies
attachingtomonarchical government are laughed
to scorn by the great Nation of the West. Now,
when Hopetoun affirms that a man who “ got
up ” in America and proposed to crown some-
body King of the United States would be
arrested by two policemen, his Excellency
makes a statement which, coming from a man
of experience and intellectual ability, would be
a monstrous lie. Coming from a gentleman who
twice failed to pass a simple military exami-
nation it is merely an honest proof of his ignor-
ance and lack of brains. True, a crank getting
up the President’s chimney, or in a church
during service, to propose any political resolu-
tion would undoubtedly be removed witli
violence like the stranger who recently
wandered into Government House, Melbourne,
and dumped himself down upon the vice-regal
sofa. But Hopetoun’s words inferred that an
American Royalist—supposing such a creature
to exist —would be in danger of imprisonment
for ‘ ‘ treason ” if he aired his ideas at a banquet.
What is the young man giving us, or, rather,
our Natives ? Herewith The Bulletin gets up
to say that Lord Hopetoun drivels, and that he
is himself a proof that Australians do not live
under a free system of government, inasmuch
as no free people would import a Governor from
the other side of the globe to make a miserable
exhibition of the fact that he knows nothing
and is incapable of concealing his ignorance.
And although we utter such sentiments without
fear of an indictment for treason, we do
solemnly record our conviction that for this
immunity from dungeon, torture, and legal
murder we are by no means indebted to persons
of the Governor class. Hopetoun’s speech to
the Natives insinuated that Australian Repub-
licans may think themselves very lucky, you
know, that they are not seized by two police-
men and punished. In short, Hopetoun is not
only a driveller, but an impudent driveller at
that.

Bradlaugh lived to know that the motion by
which he was so long kept out of his place in
Parliament had been expunged from the records
of the House. The “disreputable Atheist” of
1880 became a revered sage by efflux of time.
If Respectable Cant had a conscience, which it
hasn’t, what a number of “ old geesers ” would
blush as they remember that only ten years
have passed over their fat heads since they
thought the heavens would fall if Bradlaugh
were allowed to sit in Parliament without
kissing a Bible ! By the way, he did scramble
forward and kiss the Bible after a while. Where-
upon Cant, officially represented by the Speaker,
ruled that the kiss of an Atheist wasn’t sacred,
and Bradlaugh was cast forth into the outer
darkness to gnash his teeth for a further spell.
And now the House unanimously agrees that it
was a bigoted ass ten years ago, also, that a
man who regards swearing by the Bible as a
played-out mockery is not bound to be damned
hereafter, and that if he be damned, he will
probably find himself better off than in the
heaven of orthodox believers. The old geeser
kind of person never does think back (nor"think
forward for the matter of that) but simply
swears that everything of the moment is founded
upon glorious precedent, and cannot change for
ever and ever, amen, The present historian,
though still almost young and blooming, delights
to revive recollections of the times not so very
long ago, when Parnell, with his half-a-dozen
followers, was popularly regarded as a wild
Irish curiosity, to be greeted with parliamentary
jeers and cock-crows whenever he started to
speak. Randolph Churchill, about the same
period, was represented in the press as a
ridiculous joke, a vivacious lunatic who had
created a “ Fourth Party ” of four, just for the
fun of being called its leader. Bradlaugh—
well, lie was a disgusting blackguard, you
know, trying to get amongst a lot of gentlemen,
and being very properly slung out. Outside,
Respectable Cant always spoke of him as though
he were a political inebriate hammering at the
door of a grog-shop, and cursing “something
dreadful,” because they had refused to allow
such a degraded character on the premises.
Where is the middle-aged Tory that can deny
these hard facts lightly told?
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent who*
under the nom de plume of “ Jackass,”
draws attention to the contrast be-
tween the early Australian explorers and
Stanley, is quite right in most of the
deductions he draws, although there are
one or two instances he might have omitted.
Burke, for instance, who was fitted out with
as gorgeous a retinue almost as that of Stan-
ley, had no reason beyond his own incapa-
city for living or rather dying on nardoo.
Eyre’s famous tramp round the head of the
Great Bight was the result of his dogged deter-
mination to get through at all costs, even
though he knew that no geographical discovery
could be the result. Stuart was certainly as
fine an example of a self-reliant Australian
explorer as could be brought forward. With
but a small party and scanty outfit he reso-
lutely tackled the continent again and again,
and was really the first to cross from
ocean to ocean, for Burke simply got as
far as the head of a mangrove creek and
never saw open water. But the man who
towers above Stanley in quite heroic contrast
is Charles Sturt. Not altogether in the
physical privations undergone, for he but bore
his part therein with the rest, but in his
moral superiority to the buccaneer of Africa.
When Sturt pulled down the Murray he knew
that, if ever he returned, it would only be after
terrible toil and hardship, for there was no
hope of relief meeting him if he succeeded
in reaching the goal of his hopes, the
mouth of the river. Stuart too was in the
same predicament, but with him the difficulty
of return was not enhanced ; while with Sturt
the extra labour of returning up against the
river, compared to going down, would be,nearly
quadruple. Again, he and his party might, at
any moment, by the wreck of their boat, be
left on foot, and almost helpless in what was
then nothing but a hungry desert; from
the terrors of which Sturt had before been
forced to retire. In spite of all this
we do not find the leader exhibiting
any of the querulous peevishness, the
childish jealousy, and the contempt for native
human life that lias blotted out all Stanley’s
better qualities, whatever they might have
been. The tone breathed right throughout
his journal is one of earnest, modest desire to
fulfil his mission, to do justice to the heroism
of his comrades, and to avoid, even at the
risk of his own life, spilling the blood of the
ignorant natives. And yet this man, whose
deeds were so great, whose services to Aus-
tralia are beyond reward, whose record is so stain-
less, is left unhonoured by Australians, while
the Brigand Stanley finds friends in ”oyal
palaces. — Delcomyn.

“ The colonists knew,” says Baron Carington
at the Colonial Institution, “that England
would regard the invasion of Australia in the
same light as they would the landing of an
enemy in Kent. England would aid Australia
against a foreign foe, even if Canada objected ;
and, on the other hand, England would not
wait the consent of Australia before sending, if
required, cruisers flying the white flag to the
Bay of Fundy, or to the Behring Sea, so as to
enable colonists to feel that they belonged to
the British community. ” Which, being inter-
preted, simply means that England wouldn’t
hesitate to breathe fire and slaughter in the
Bay of Fundy at the expense of involving Aus-
tralia in a war, about the merits of which the
latter country might know and care nothing,
but which would all the same serve as an invi-
tation to a host of flying Yankee pirates to
come and stick us up. Suppose, -after England
had sent her cruisers to the Bay of Fundy and
declared war against the States, it became plain
to the world that J. Bull was in the wrong,
how could Bull aforesaid expect the Austra-
lians to sympathise with him as against the
Yankees, who, as we are reminded by Imperial
officials at every Fourth of July banquet, are
also part of “ the great Anglo-Saxon com-
munity ? ”

“ Give the Australian Chief Justices life-peer-
ages,” says ex-Governor Carington —who him-
self is an hereditary lord and not a mere life-peer.
Baron Smith thus considers the magnates of the
Australian bench unfit to be admitted into the
sacred circle of born “nobility,” of which he
himself is a lustrous ornament, or else he re-
gards the whole system of hereditary nobility
as a sham. Leaving the Baron to wriggle on
the horns of this dilemma, we may proceed to
remark that the two largest-minded and most
popular men now on the Australian bench,
plain George Higinbothan and Sir Charles
Lilley are both loudly-avowed Republicans,
and that the former would not accept a title
even if it carried with it a million a year. The
Australian democratic idea does not include
“ My Lord Chief Justice Darley,” but it cer-
tainly does include stripping Chief Justice
Darley and Chief Justice Way and all the rest
of .the Chief Justices of their wigs and scarlet
robes and other imported upholstery Austra-
lia has admiringly heard of President Andrew
Jackson who, after his election as President of
the United States, rode unattended and in a
grey tweed suit to Congress on his old white
horse and hitched it up to the gate; and who when
he had made his speech in Congress quietly
walked out and unhitched the old white horse
again.

Pericles and Parnell—A Parallel.
Few would venture to compare the condition
of Athens under the rule of Pericles, when
there were displayed an intellectual activity
and a progress in art unexampled in the world's
history, to the condition of Ireland under the
rule of the “uncrowned King,” when ruin,
desolation and poverty brooded over the land ;
and yet the similarity not only in the principles
and policy of these great leaders of men, but
even in the minuthe of their character, is truly
remarkable. Both sprung front aristocratic
families, deserted the class to which they
belonged, and placed themselves at the head of
the people : they chose rather to solicit the
favour ot the multitude and the poor than
of the rich and the few, Pericles,
like Parnell, was cold and reserved
and shrank from publicity and popularity ; ho
avoided all social entertainments and recrea-
tions, believing, like the Irish leader, that,
“freedom of entertainments takes away all
distinction of office, and that dignity is but
little consistent with familiarity.” It is but
necessary to make a few extracts from the
translation of Plutarch's “Life of Pericles”
to show the remarkable parallel that exists
between the leader of Athenian democracy
during the days of Athenian supremacy and
that “very remarkable man” (as Gladstone
called him a few years ago) who during the last
ten years has guided the. destinies of a down-
trodden and oppressed people ;
Pericles took care not to make his person cheap among
the people, and appeared among them only at proper
intervals ; nor did he speak to all points that were debated
before them, but reserved himself for greater occasions ;
despatching business of less consequence by other orators
with whom he had an intimacy.
Substitute Parnell for Pericles and every-
one conversant with the politics of Ireland will
admit that this extract gives an exact account
of the policy which Parnell has followed from
the day when he was first elected leader of the
Irish party to the time when cant split his
party and left him but .'l5 parliamentary fol-
lowers.
Such was the solicitude of Pericles when he had to
speak in public that he always first addressed a prayer to
the gods that not a word might unawares escape him un-
suitable to the occasion.
History does not record that Parnell offered
up such a prayer before bis speeches in West-
minster, but we have it on the authority of the
G.O.M. that he had the power of saying exactly
what he meant and that he possessed a thorough
grasp of his ideas and wants. Mr, Gladstone
once said of him :
No man, as far as I can judge, is more successful than
the hon. member in doing that which it. is commonly sup-
posed that all speakers do, but which in my opinion few
really do—and I do not include myself among these few—
namely, in saying what he means to say.
Thucydides represents the administration of
Pericles as favouring aristocracy, and tells us
that, though the government was called demo-
cratic, it was really in, the hands of one who
had engrossed the whole authority. Can it not
with truth be said ol Parnell, oft dubbed
Dictator, that he, too, engrossed the whole
authority, and often left his aspiring lieutenants
in ignorance o| his policy and programme ?
They accused him of an intrigue with the wife of
Mexippcs, his friend and lieutenant in the army.
Mutatw muUmdix this, too, will apply to the
“ discrowned king.”
The Lacedaemonians, persuaded that if they could
remove Pericles out of the way they should be better
able to manage the Athenians, required them to banish
all execrable persons from among them ; ajid Pericles.
was by his mother’s side related to those that were pro-
nounced execrable in the affair of Cylon.
How curiously like the present state of things
in Ireland, when Respectable Cant tries to
hopnd ou,t of public life the “ execrable ” Par-
nell, in order that it may be better able to
mapicLge the Ipish ?
There is always a good show in. Australia for
t,he well-dressed malefactor who snuffles when
he is run in, and brings evidence to prove that
he went wrong from the mos’t pious motives.
For instance, at Daylesford (Vic.) a plain and
consistent Christian w;as hauled up, the other
day concerning two separate assaults on a
young lady, aged fifteen, and was convicted on
both charges. In, fact, he acknowledged the
whole affair witjh a meek, cherubic snule, and
explained that he only assaulted the girl to
“ ascertain whether she was a person of good
character whom, he could safely allow to asso-
ciate with his own daughter,” and the Bench,
in consideration of this noble sentiment, let
him off with a fine of £lO. Apparently the
Daylesford beaks think it quite a reasonable
thing that a Christian inhabitant should go
round assaulting his daughter’s female friends
to find out if their characters are perfectly cor-
rect. And yet, if a bricklayer is charged, upon
v ery shady evidence, with molesting any kind
of a female in order to ascertain whether lie can
associate with her without injury to his morals,
what a thundering row ensues right off!

The New South Wales Supremo Court will: be
closed until 16th February, when the first term
of 1891 will open. The judges have had
holiday vacation since 23rd December last. The
number of remanets since last year and the
accumulation of legal work during the long-
drawn-out vacation will demand good manage-
ment, apd vigorous work at the hands of the
judges, as only three working weeks are
allowed, —for jury trials— i.e. , for clearing off'
the arrears of last year and the present accumu-
lation before the Sittings cease and the judges
go on circuit. Many cases awaiting trial must
therefore go over to the June Sittings, nearly
six months from the present time and about
nine months from commencement of the action !
Affairs in the District Court arc in a correspond-
ingly unsatisfactory state.

“ The Idler,” albeit it was not hailed on
first night with the extraordinary enthusiasm that
a bright play written by an Australian might
expect, has caught on at Sydney Garrick. The
story is of one Mark Cross (name suggestive of
illiteracy) who hopefully returns from burying a
wicked wife abroad to find the girl his position
had prevented him from wooing, the wife of
another. He then discovers that he had formerly
known her husband, Sir John Harding (Ed. Sass)
as “Euchre Jack,” and reminds him how the law
has not relaxed its efforts to capture him for the
slaughter of a man in a drunken Californian
brawl. Curtain. In the next aot, Cross sools
Simeon Strong (Laurence Cautley) brother
of the murdered man on to Harding, acquaints
Lady Harding (Olga Nethersole) with her
husband’s danger and makes her complaisance
the price of Harding’s safety. Curtain.
Then he works on Strong’s gratitude (he had once
saved his life) to obtain the emancipation of Hard-
ing ; this secured, he attempts to trade it away
with his friend’s wife in exchange for her virtue.
Her purity and fearlessness save her, but she has
not time to escape ere her husband, coming to
consult Cross, discovers her fan, denounces and
deserts her. Curtain. In the final act Harding
compels Cross to face him with pistols, but they
are interrupted by Strong, the American, who
gets between them in a manner which lacks the
dramatic quality of abruptness. Theotheisrush in
and explanation is followed by reconciliation. Then
Cross is patted on the back by Strong and
requested to take command of an Arctic expedi-
tion. He wavers and, left alone, meditates suicide,
but, checked by the sight of his mother’s portrait,
refrains and orders his valet to pack up. “ Where
for, sir ?” says Hans Phillips (he got a big recep-
tion on first night). “ For the North Pole ”is the
reply, and the curtain goes down on an anti-
climax. The author has clumsily interpolated a
comedy underplot from which all the lighting-up
effects are eliminated by the heavy-wet Merry-
weather of Harold Russell and the cold, cute
self-consciousness of Mary Kingsley, who is not
built after the ingenue pattern. Haddon Chambers
is evidently a close student of Sardou, for “The
Idler,” distinctly French in style, at once
suggests “ La Tosca ” and half-a-dozen of the
latter’s masterpieces. Its weak points are a
straining after Rochefoucauld ism, and a crude-
ness chiefly made evident in the want of homo-
geneity apparent in characterisation. Cross, for
an instance, a mean and contemptible cur as ever
lived, is, apparently, accounted rather a magnani-
mous if misguided person by his creator, and the
whole crowd do and say things totally at variance
with any previously-formed conceptions of their
dispositions. Cross is a very difficult part to
play. Cartwright -certainly* didn’t look it,
nevertheless, he managed to smooth over
its incongruities and infamies in a wonderful
style. Olga Nethersole was equal to every re-
quirement of a not excessively exacting part.
Like many others we did not like the boldness
with which she ratified the assignation by tossing
her bouquet to the feet of her tempter. \Ve think
the struggle between fear for her husband’s safety
and her own, virtue, would have been more
artistically displayed by a gradual loosening of
her grasp on the flowers and their gentle fall to
the ground. Emma Cwyne is realistic as the
man-hunting widow ; Jenny Watt-Tanner as the
motherly, good-natured one. We nearly forgot
to mention the Harding of Ed. Sass, a character
run somewhat on Lord Jura lines, and ably
handled ; also the Strong of Cautley, an anything
but impressive performance. After Friday week
the company skip to Melbourne Royal, where
they open in “The Fortune of War,” a specta-
cular drama. The vacancy at the Carriok will be
filled by the Turners in opera.

“Comrades,” now running at Sydney
Criterion, seems scarcely an improvement on the
weird wail that preceded it. As our knowledge
of the piece is confined to a brief sniff at the pro-
gramme ere departing in haste to view sundry

closing. Next week we’ll have time to say some-

“ Arrah-na-Pogue,” up for six nights only,
is staged and scene-painted as well at Sydney
Royal as if intended to run for a month. It is
also fairly well cast, though Robert Inman as the
C’Crady and Constance Deorwyn as Fanny
Power are ill-suited. Everyone was agreeably
surprised by the Arrah of Miss Appleton. She
won the audience first with her good looks, and
held them captive by her tender sweetness and
utter want of staginess throughout. The Michael
Feeney of Charles Seagrave is a good perform-
ance on the conventional lines, and the Kitty
Walsh of Maggie Oliver (always reminiscent of
the other Maggie); the. Beamish M'Coul of
“Bob” Stewart, are all right. Nevertheless the
show hasn’t taken Sydney by storm, and it might
have been better policy, financially considered, to
keep on the pantomime. “Daddy” Lehr. who
represents the “Poguey Man” in front, informs
us that the “Irish Detective,” as previously an-
nounced, goes up on Saturday night .

Theatrical managers don’t seem to under-
stand human nature. In pantomime all the
women are made up to look like houris, while the
men partake of the complexion of satyrs. This is
good biz. for the women on the stage, bad for
women off; good for the men in the audience,
bad for the men on the boards. Women go to
theatres to feast their eyes on the beautiful, just
as men do; gazing only on the lovely of their own
.'lex they soon get cloyed. An American writer
complains of the thin arms and thick legs of t h ■
ballet. “ Pads !” says he. That does not follow ;
the feminine leg, in most cases, is fully developed
by rope-skipping, rinking, dancing, block-walk-
ing, while the arms never know exercise or
useful employment —the man does all the
hugging. All this reminds us that the panto-
mime is still running at Her Majesty’s to ex-
cellent houses. The professional matinee of
Saturday was highly successful, and at the impe-
cunious children’s gathering of Monday, gallery
and family circle were filled to overflowing, and
hundreds turned away.

Manager Larmour’s performing seal, at
Coogee Aquarium, considerably disturbed the
bathers there by darting in among them the other
evening. Afterwards, he scared the ladies by
waltzing over the dance-floor on his fantastic fin,
and closed an exciting day by clambering up a
20-foot wall and flopping heavily into the roadway
on the chest of an apple-woman. Had he fol-
lowed this programme on the eve of the publicans'
picnic, lots would have yelled: “ Fan tods : got
’em again ! ” Donovan and Parker fight here on
Saturday week for the mounted sword champion-
ship on the lawn at 3 p.m. The dances of Wednes-
day evenings continue to boom.

At Sydney School of Arts a complete
change of programme and the accession of Phoebe
Levy as a skipping-rope dancer and, in conjunc-
tion with Anselmo Flores, as a guitar and mando-
line duettist, keeps things humming. It. C.
Stynes handles the dominoes in masterly fashion,
and Fred. Norris, the skatist, falls over the foot-
lights into the laps of fat ladies nightly.
At Bondi Aquarium Professor Fernandez
has made two very successful ascensions and
earth-leaps. That of the moonlight night was
especially beautiful. The balloon went straight
up out of the little glen with the skyward rush of
a rocket, till it caught the wind, when it drifted
towards Coogee. Then the Professor, who was
hanging by his heels distributing bills, struck a
fusee, re-lit an extinguished lamp that was pen-
dant from the parachute, and shortly afterwards
made his jump. At first he shot downwards like
a falling star, then, when the parachute opened,
hung for a brief space m mid-air clearly silhouetted
against the skyline and came gently to earth.

J. L. Toole is fading away like a flower at
Melbourne Opera House, and the production of a
series of comedies for one successive night or so
fails to attract much attention. No more does
Melbourne evince a burning desire to seek the
bubble reputation even at the pay-box’s mouth.
Altogether, the Prince of Wales’ pal has
played ten weeks on the banks of Yarra Water,
and he is now not caviare but “dry hash ” to the
general. The Bulletin takes no credit to itself
for having foretold J. L. TVs inability to last out
a long season in any Australian city, firstly when
his engagement was announced, and secondly
after he had started at the Princess’s. London in
the bulk is not much addicted to patronising “ the
little theatre in the Strand ” (and an uncommonly
little one it is) where Toole runs his little show for
jaded aristocrats, sporting acquaintances, and
those worthy people who take him on his news-
paper reputation, and are pretty easily amused.
The latter start laughing even before their idol
puts in an appearance. The exquisite humour of
his knock at the door senfls them into convulsions.
New-chums who have snorted at adverse criti-
cisms upon this overrated artist are for the
most part very young men who knew “ Toole ”
only as a name at “ home,” and have no idea of
the fortunate combination of circumstances by
which he has been enabled so long to figure, off
the boards, as the most excruciatingly funny man
of the English-speaking world. He fairly earned
a reputation in the old days, since when, good
luck, good tact, and the social effects of a good
heart, have relieved “Johnny” of the necessity
of moving with the times. Apropos to the fare-
well banquets tendered to Toole by somebodies*
and would-be somebodies, before he left England,
the Referee wondered whether Australians
would expect to find in the actor histrionic talent
commensurate with the fuss made about his depar-
ture, and, if so, what the Australian public would
think of Toole when they got him. After getting
a good deal of him, they seem scarcely to think
about him at all. Nevertheless, he is a far better
comedian than many Melbourne folks, who cannot
disassociate the star from his feeble satellites,
seem to imagine. In the “ Spitalfields Weaver ”
and many other old-fashioned farces, again as
Mawworm and similar characters of the oily
antique kind, J. L. Toole wants a lot of beating
even now. And we maintain this notwithstanding
that Albert Edward’s devoted admirer desires to
have The Bulletin “suppressed.” Believing
itself to be as honest, well-meaning, and a little
more humorous than Toole, The Bulletin has
no desire to see him suppressed. On the contrary,
this magnanimous journal trusts that lie will long
live to play small practical jokes and cultivate by
degrees those radical principles which would do
him such a lot of good. Toole takes his farewell
on Friday, as the Artful Dodger and a few other
things :
On Saturday (7th) the Gourlay-Snazelle

As for “Dr. Bill,” that retired rake is
filling the Bijou everywhere and always. Anson"'
contortions, when he learns that his better,
stouter self is locked in the bath-room with a male
person of pedigree unknown, are alone worth the
money. Miss Myra Kemble, too, works well
throughout the second act, and Brough throws in
at least one unmistakeable touch of nature. His
anxious exhortation of Myra to “pull herself
together, ” as she lies fainting on the sofa, carries
one back in imagination to Bill’s bachelor days.
He seems to speak as one accustomed to see fair
creatures “go off” unexpectedly, and the old
guilty dread that his companion may possibly
start screaming, is perceptible in the tremor of
his voice, “Pull yourself together”—how often
has he said the same thing, in his snug little
study, with its Turkey carpet, inviting couch in
the corner, and curtains drawn close !
The giddy ladies’ doctor.
Afraid that he has shock’d her,
Uncertain of his patient, and how far he ought
to go,
Cries “Pull yourself together ! ”
The while he wonders whether
The pretty dear has fainted, or is shamming,
doneherknow.

At the Alexandra they may be able to
hold back “The Miner’s Right” for another
week, inasmuch as “The Black Elag” flourishes
considerably. Darnpier’s fight with four warders
is not so desperate an encounter as it might be,
but the violence with which those miserable myr-
midons fall over without due provocation is, on
the whole, a qualifying mystery. Besides, the
prisoner’s escape from Portland is the making of
the “ Flag.” It brings about a wonderfully quick
change of scene, and perhaps the best representa-
tion of a boat tossing on the waves that has been
seen in these parts. The mechanical rocking in
the cradle of the deep is most ingeniously managed.

All three halls are tenanted just now. At
the Gaiety they have “fifty handsome ladies,’’
more or less, and a burlesque of the Wild West.
The minstrels at the Vic. have been strengthened
by a Wonder Worker and a Witch, while Madame
Cora, described as Australia’s Favourite, has
returned from a long, weary tour of the world
to practise illusions and mesmerism at St. George’s
Hall. Madame’s illusions are mostly of long
standing and undoubted respectability, but her
mesmerism looks uncommonly thin at the first
hasty glance. The Waxworks is doing well with
the improved loud Telephone, and the Battle of
Waterloo is taking place around the road to
Brussels, as usual.

Sydney D.T. theatrical criticism :
Wonderfully well dressed in a richly-flowered gown of
sulphur-coloured silk, this Lady Harding seemed fit ex-
cuse tor the wildest passion.
Perhaps the D.T. critic’s experience has been
exceptional, but wo have generally found that wild
passion—that is to say, we don’t know anything
about it personally, but we have been credibly
informed the ladies who excited that sort of feel-
ing succeeded best without—in short, that won-
derfully well-dressed women—hang it, we mean
that it wasn’t usually the sulphur-coloured silk
that did it.
Good-bye, J.L.T.,
May you meet no gales
As you cross the sea
To join the Prince of Wales;
Should he ask—“ The Press
Exalted you, I trust ? ”
Reply—“ Well, I confess
The Bulletin was Just.
“ It sometimes gave me jam,
And sometimes marmalade.
And sometimes it said ‘dam/
I’m very much afraid.'
But O ! 1 do declare
It prints some awful things,
And doesn't seem to care
A little bit for kings.
So bitter are its sneers
At those I dearly love;
It doesn’t look on peers
As angels from above ;
It never has been taught
To reverence the Crown—
I really think you ought
To put that paper down.”
Good-bye, Johnny dear,
Remember us to Turn;
He will be glad to hear
You made a tidy sum.
We wish you well indeed,
And, with a friendly grin,
Exhort you still to read
The good old Bulletin.
At Poverty Point.
Says an ingenuous and socially ill-informed Now Zea-
lander ; —“Madame Marian Burton, the much-admired
vocalist now in Auckland, is a married lady and her hus-
band travels with her. It may he a further shock to the
‘chappies’ who are so hopelessly in love with the beauti-
ful singer to learn that she is now in that condition in
which ladies love to be who love their lords. Madame
Burton has resolved, after the present brief concert
season is over, to remain in Auckland until after her con-
finement, which will take place within a few weeks.”
What will they say in England?

Boohsa, the great eccentric harpist, died in Sydney 35
years back, after running away with Anna Bishop, and
breaking Sir Henry Bishop’s heart. But Anna’s last visit
to Australia was as Madame Schultz, her hubby an ugly,
iron-grey-hair man, in a fur cap, with that combination
of grey hair and black moustache, which is the correct
make up for Brigard in Frou Frou. Of course Boohsa
was ugly as sin, with a mug like a springbok, or buoh-
billi, and of course Sir ’Enry was a very fine man—May
and December. Bochsa didn’t survive him long, but
Anna did a very long time—time to get shipwrecked
often.

The French Government have given Sardou’s new
drama, “Thermidor,” a prodigious reclame, by ordering
the expurgation of passages reflecting on Robespierre,
the Incorruptible’s memory being cherished by the Reds.
The play is wondrously exciting, about that famous day
in Thermidor when Robespierre was overthrown through
the rebellion led by Tallien. There is a woman every-
where, and all Robespierre’s democratic and sanguinary
enthusiasm arose from his being jilted by Mdlle.
Deshortis, a wealthy aristocratic heiress, in Arras, where
he came from. Another woman procured his fall,
Madame Tallien, then Countess de Fontenay. She egged
on Tallien to head the conspiracy against Robespierre,
who had resolved on killing Tallien, while he kept the
Countess in prison. Tallien saved her at Bordeaux, but
she ultimately deserted him for the Banker Ouvrard, and
then married the Belgian Prince de Chimay. We are
just on the Centenary of the crisis in the French Revo-
lution.
There is something light and refreshing about this
little idea. In Madame Tussaud’s Waxworks the latest
attraction is a facsimile of the sitting-room of Mrs.
Pearcey—recently hanged for the murder of a woman and
an infant —with pictures, couch, piano, and everything
complete. Also the murderess is thrown in without any
extra charge, her duplicate in wax being represented in a
standing position in the centre of the apartment. The
blood-stained perambulator, in which the corpse was
removed, is likewise on exhibition, together with the
jacket worn by the prisoner the day she demolished her
adversary, models of the heads of the victims, and the
piece of toffee which the baby was sucking when Mrs.
Pieroey suddenly wiped it out with the household axe.
In fact, the only thing that is wanting is the milk
that the same infant would have drunk next day if it had
survived.
Dick Stewart, Nellie’s dad, still adorns the Melbourne
pave, coming up brisk and smiling. He was certainly
a remarkably good actor at the time when he made such a
wonderful Dundreary to Jefferson, in “ Our American
Cousin,” and followed it up with an entire contrast as his
Uncle Pete, in “ The Octoroon,” but latterly he got very
mannered, so that his left leg was thrust into all his
parts, like Charley Mathews’.
One of Sothern’s least unpleasant practical jokes was a
simple mystification, which took the victim thoroughly
aback, but for others he ought to have been kicked.
Well, in this case he began by getting an envelope sent
all round England, until it was covered with the names of
post towns, being passed on from one friend of his to
another, with pencil addresses. Then he addressed the
envelope in ink to a fashionable stranger in London, with
an invitation to dine—three months old—whereupon the
stranger pitched into the P.O. authorities for their
stupidity.

disastrous failure. First the company were suddenly
moved out of the Opera House ; then they closed the
Theatre Royal, and went Aquarium concerting; finally
Emma and her Carroll left for Sydney. The company
stayed in Brisbane.

an incarnation of all the domestic virtues that she is
never happy when her husband is out of sight. “Is it
because she loves him so much?” said someone. “No,”
said the other fellow ; “because she knows him so well.”
John R. Rogers says of his domestic troubles that “her
mother’s at the bottom of it all 1”

A party of theatrical people were entering the vestibule
of an Australian theatre one evening, when someone,
viewing their numbers, said interrogatively to a pressman
who stood by: “ Good circle to-night ? ” “ Emery paper,”
was the concise reply.
The Australian “ bogus manager ” is again on the war
path. This time he has victimised a pantomime crowd.
In some countries, notably Germany and Scotland, says
the Scots Observer, the folk-songs everybody knows and
sings are brimming with melody and passion as any words
that ever came from the human breast; but in England
their room is filled by the scandalous or drivelling min-
strelsy of the music hall “ artiste ” and the burnt-cork
nigger. Why and whence the difference ! Whence and
why but that the first two nations have feelings and
capacities which the third has not.
