The Henley-Stevenson play, “ Beau Austin,” produced
at London Haymarket on two Monday nights by Beer-
bohm Tree in pursuance of his design to try new plays
after this fashion, was hawked about among the various
English managers for years ere it got this show, and was
played in America, unsuccessfully, under another title.
It is brimful of tears, and its names were, apparently,
selected from a line in Scott’s fine ballad of “ Young
Loohinvar for every character, bar “the Beau,” has been
christened either Foster, Fenwick or Musgrave. It is
suggested that it would be more appropriately named,
“May a Man Marry his Mistress?” with the sub-
title : “ May a Woman accept Matrimonial Re-
paration from her Betrayer ? ” According to the
authors the answer to both question is: “Yes
—if it be shown that the man truly loves the vyoman,
that he is prepared to suffer shame and humiliation for
the outward maintenance of her honour, and if it is some-
thing more than a belated effort of that
prompts his action.” Critics say that “ Beau Austin ”is
superior in merit to ninety-nine successful plays out of
every hundred, but altogether without acting dramatic
interest. Therefore, Beerbohm Tree’s statement on open-
ing night that in staging this and kindred works “We
shall not consider the commercial aspect of the case of
the first importance ” has its consolations.
Signor and Signora Majeroni arrived in Sydney from
Hongkong by the Chang-Sha last week. Both, everyone
will be sorry to hear, have been seriously ill, indeed,
Majeroni has lost nearly three stone in weight since
leaving Australia. This loss of weight is, no doubt to an
extent, a consequence of the anxiety and worry caused
by the serious condition of the Signora, who caught
pleurisy at Agra, while they were viewing the interior
after a highly successful Indian season. Somewhat
restored by the attentions at Bombay of Dr. Sydney
Smith, the great specialist, the Majeronis then visited
Singapore, Hongkong and Shanghai, where the Signor
played to big houses, assisted by amateurs. Signora
Majeroni, though otherwise restored, is unfortunately
deprived of her voice, through paralysis of a vocal chord,
for the time, but Dr. Piaschi expects that complete rest
will restore it eventually.
J. J. Foster, manager for Henry George, Bemenyi and
others in the colonies, is giving society concerts with the
latter in London. Among other notabilities catered for
he mentions the Duke of Cambridge, the Ear! of
Londesborough, Colonel North (the hitrate King), the
Prince of Wales, and many of the West End clubs ; also
at the German Athenaeum, Les Bon Freres, the Lyric,
Meistersingers and Grosvenor “ Ladies’ nights” meeting
with great success. Mr. Foster has secured a six weeks’
guarantee engagement for Hungary, M. Remenyi’s native
country, opening in Buda-Pesth, the capital, on 14th
Feb. ; also a 25 weeks guarantee from the Redpath
Bureau for America, commencing in New York on 15th
Sept. next. The gap between these two engagements will
be filled up touring Continental Europe. During Mr.
Foster’s stay in London he has been made an F.R.G.S. in
return for his valuable geographical contributions to that
Society.
Charles Dillon, the elder, used to relate an amusing
experience he had when in Australia. He was due in a
certain city which boasted two theatres, the managers of
which had been in treaty to secure him for the same date.
He closed with one. After a tedious journey he arrived.
The first thing that attracted his attention was a huge,
burly man blazing with jewellery, and shouting “ Which
is Charles Dillon ?” The actor timidly introduced himself.
“Thank Heaven ! I was afraid you wouldn’t come, and
isn’t the other manager riled ;he tried hard for ye. What
do you think he’s going to do to spite me? Why, play
‘The Ticket-of-Leave Man.’ ” “Is that such a big draw,
then?” “Draw be hanged! No. It was arranged
between us that the confounded piece should never be
done ; but he’s going to play it because he knows I am
an old lag myself !”
Henry Irving once received a rather severe snub from
the elder Dillon. The latter was playing a very unprofit-
able engagement at “The Lane,” while Irving was on the
full tide of success at the Lyceum"with “Hamlet.” It
was “ Treasury Day,” and several of Dillon’s pals—most
of them old-timers—surrounded him in the private bar of
the Wellington Tavern, which was next to the Lyceum.
Enter Irving—“ Glass of sherry, please.” While drinking
it he observed Dillon. Approaching with a respectful yet
confident air, said he: “ Good day, Mr. Dillon—fine
weather, eh?” Dillon quietly surveyed him with a who-
the-devil-are-you gaze. “ Perhaps you don’t recognise
me. j—er—played Horatio to your Hamlet at the Theatre
Royal, Manchester, er—er —twelve years ago.” Dillon
pondered with a pompous, puzzled air for some time. At
last, seeming to recollect: “Yes! yes! of course—l re-
member now*. Let me see—your name’s Irving—yes, yes.
Doing anything now ?”
Says Nym Crinkle, in discussing the reasons that gave
Mary Anderson a leading place on the boards“ With
measureless advantages of arms and voice and stature,
Mary Anderson has never warmed anything. Her mis-
sion was horatory. She came to us with the untamed
vibration of the Kentucky presiding elder, and the first
thing she did was to preach Pauline at the Fifth Avenue
Theatre, and fill the parquet with mourners’ benches.
She had imbibed the singular notion in the West that
histrionism consisted in using a contralto voice below the
staff, and in telegraphing her ideas with her arms. She
was so unlike any other artist that the world had ever
seen that we got to calling her ‘ our Mary,’ in recognition
of her proud distinction.” Another writer, taking up
the parable, declares that “Mary was superb, like a
monolith, with something of that object’s crystal hard-
ness. But, unlike Cleopatra’s Needle, no storms could
affect her surface. She was stately, oratorical,
statuesque; and some, who had examined her more
minutely than others, said she bad a white soul. She
was superior, impressive, emphatic. Her carriage was so
ample it drove over rhetoric like an old-fashioned post
coach, and the critics swung on her gait like truant
schoolboys.”
This is how they keep cool in the Bowery, New York.
On a calico sign over a dirty doorway you read: “ Great
bathing panorama. You get a bath and a breeze insidfe
for ten cents. Coney Island outdone. Nymphs, mermaids,
and real women disporting themselves in real water. The
greatest sight of the century.” You enter a long, low
room crowded with a sweltering mob, whose eyes are
fixed on the little stage that fills one end of the tunnel-
like apartment. There, a strip of badly-daubed border
represents the sky, and a jagged piece of pasteboard the
laughing ocean, while two large fans that give no breeze
creak noisily from the wings. Behind the ground-border,
which barely conceals their calves, a number of women
wearing a single are cutting capers in the
water and throwing pulverised salt over one another’s
shoulders, in the fashion of girls that splash about at the
real sea. After a series of evolutions too peculiar and
emphatic to permit of careful description, a whale, not
quite so large as the bathers, floated into view, and, amid
screams of fright, the monster opened his jaws, and the
most succulent young woman of the gay group w’as
eno-ulphed—all except her limbs, which protruded from
the whale’s lips and kicked violently as the curtain fell
over the graphic picture.

Pantomime, although apparently appreciated, doesn’t
seem to pay in Brisbane where, the other day, there was
an awful burst-up with “The Forty Thieves” crowd at
the Theatre Royal. From published accounts it seems
that Frank Beverley appealed to Frank Whyte, who held
a mortgage over the show, for his screw. The coin not
being forthcoming the comedian proceeded to punch
Whyte in the street. Sequel: Police court case, and
numerous sensational pars, for nothing.
There can be no two opinions on the accuracy of the
late Dion Boucicault’s assertion that the dramatist, unlike
the poet, is evolved out of experience, and is not a born
genius. The main difficulty in theatrical authorship lies
not, as is generally supposed, in the elaboration of an in-
teresting plot, but in its rendering into concise and fluent
dialogue: or, in a word, in reproducing the entirety of a
novel in a condensed form without losing an atom of the
flavour. That this task requires much practical training
unquestionable.

It is ludicrous to see a Sydney evening-
paper publishing as original an obviously Ameri-
can short story; also, a three-quarter column
humorous sketch, written for and published in,
likewise paid for by The Bulletin months ago--
and certainly not written by the man who has the
cast-iron impudence now to fasten his name to it.
in justice to ourselves, who practically inaugurated
the system of paying for original matter in
Sydney, we mean in future to show, on every
possible occasion, where these unblushing pirates
get their “ original ” matter from.
It doesn’t matter one dump whether the
press is or is not admitted to the sittings of the
coming Federal Convention. Given reporters and
daily press reports —we should say, versions—of
the proceedings, every gasometer m the Conven-
tion would deem it his duty to supply the country
with murk on the question of Federation. On
the other hand, in a secret conclave the weak-
minded men would be hypnotised by the stronger-
Wl lled—or small cliques would be overmatched by
large cabals—and National Australia would run
the risk of being both botched and betrayed.
The sensible and safe course —whether the sittings
be private or public—is to swear in four or five
Parliamentary shorthand-writers, bind them by
oath to take verbatim reports of all the speeches,
and let their reports, unaltered, be published and
circulated, with an affidavit from the phono-
graphers that the same have not been tampered
with. However, the final result of the Conven-
tion will depend not so much on what is said in
formal conference as on what Parkes says to
Gillies and Gillies says to Parkes in the private
sitting-room at the hotel.
With the fullest knowledge of the dormant
demon they are rousing, the banded capitalists of
Australia are vindictively attempting to crush out
trades-unionism and to enslave labour. The
Pastoralists’ Association is only one feeler of the
great octopus, Capital. Behind the squatters
stand the shipowners, the Employers’ Association,
and millions of money. On the side of the
shearers are the pence of the people and hundreds
of thousands of the people themselves. _ Giddy
with the pride of recent victory, Capital is ruth-
lessly forcing the tight further, and selects the
shearers as a nomadic, .san.s-franchise, and pre-
sumably weak wing of the Labour Army. If we
can believe any official report of the pastoralists,
months of organising have enabled them to engage
a number of blacklegs—pirate shearers—with
whom, by timing localities with the varying
seasons, it is proposed to shear all the sheep on
the continent, from the Gulf of Carpen-
taria to that of St. Vincent. Ten thou-
sand unionist shearers in Queensland are
told that there is to be for them no bread-
winning unless they choose to bend the knee to
thesauatter. The old transparent fraud, “Law
and Order,” is again presented as a shield to cover
Capital’s tyranny. The squatters “have made
arrangements with the Queensland Government
to supply sufficient police protection for their free
labourers.” That is to say, the squatters have
hired mercenaries from the Government to shoot
down fellow-citizens robbed of their means of
living. In vain have the labour organisations
asked for “an open conference.” The squatters
proffer a false thing which they call a conference,
“ provided * freedom of contract ’ be recognised.”
“ Freedom of contract ” is the hall-mark of wage-
slavery. The new squatting agreement “seeks to
make labour submit to reductions of wages, in
some items the reduction being 83 percent.” The
squatters are distinctly on the war-path, and
brutally challenge the retaliation of the fire-stick,
of personal violence and bloodshed, hoping to
entrap the shearers into illegal mistakes. On the
other hand, the piracies and robberies of labour
by squatterdom are to be upheld by the batons
and revolvers of hireling mercenaries, chartered
from and liveried by the State.

“The Great Pro-Consul.”
SKETCHES FROM A LIFE OF ACTION.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
11.
When Governor Grey landed in Auckland
on 14th November, 1845, he found the whole of
the settlers in a state of consternation. The
country people, dreading a war of races, had
flown to the town as a place of refuge, though
with small reliance on its security, as the dreaded
Heke was expected to attack it shortly. Brito-
marte barracks were entrenched, two block houses
erected, Fort Ligar thrown up near the Roman
Catholic chapel, and the windows of St. Daubs
Church barricaded.
With disorganised finances, a bankrupt treasury,
and a defeated soldiery, the position looked
desperate.
The Governor took the field in person and
assumed the command of the forces, and within
two months of his arrival in New Zealand he had
brought the war to an end, retrieved the disasters
in the field, forced the enemy to sue for peace,
restored the credit of the colony, and placed its
finances on a proper footing.
* * *
But though Heke and the Northern
Maoris were pacified there were troubles ahead in
the Company's Settlement at Wellington likely to
be more formidable and more difficult to overcome
than the war in the North. The manner in which
Captain Grey out the Gordian knot and suddenly
stilled the ferment has been more persistently
misrepresented than any episode of his first
Governorship.
The warlike chiefs, Rangihaeata and Ra ipa-
raha, were bent on war, though the latter craftily
concealed his designs, and even declared his
intentions to be friendly.
Early in April, 1846, an old man named Gilles-
pie and his son were murdered by a Maori taua
(war party) at the Hutt, near Wellington. Rau
paraha indignantly denounced the deed, offered
to assist in capturing the murderers, and even
went on board H.M.S.S. Driver to see the
Governor, who had at once proceeded in the ship to
Porirua Harbour, close to the wily old chief’s
principal stronghold.
But Rauparaha had meanwhile taken care that
the culprits had got away into the interior.
Attacks continued to be made on settlers, ambus-
cades were laid by the Maoris, small parties of
soldiers were attacked and several killed in differ-
ent places, and the Governor soon became con-
vinced that Rangihaeta and Rauparaha were
acting in concert, but it was only when he had
received positive proof, of this secret understanding
that he determined on the bold stroke which
paralysed the enemy, and has since been adduced
against Sir George as an evidence of treachery and
bad faith.
The following is his account of the transaction :
Before daybreak on the morning of the 22nd
July, 1846, while he was in bed at Wellington, a
man rode up hastily to the house and, rousing the
porter, desired immediate audience of tire Gover-
nor on a matter of the most urgent importance.
Captain Grey at once got up and saw the man,
who proved to be a Wanganui settler with whom
he Was acquainted and could depend on. This
settler lived on very friendly terms with the
Maoris, and his tale was that on the night of the
19th, after being out cattle-hunting all day,
being benighted, he went to the whare <*f
a chief with whom he was acquainted, and lay
down for a sleep. That while there emissaries
from Rauparaha and Rangihaeata had come in
and invited the co-operation of the Wanganui
tribes in a combined attack on the Wellington
settlement. This was agreed on, and the march
was to take place on the following day. His pre-
sence was not perceived, or perhaps they thought
either that he did not understand what was going
on, or was asleep, as indeed he pretended to be.
He remained quietly on his mat all night, seemen
perfectly at ease in the morning, had a meal with
his hosts, and then mounting his horse rode in a
homeward direction to deceive them.
Then, getting another horse, he rode deliberately
towards the North as though still cattle-hunting,
but when quite out of sight turned and made for
Wellington, keeping well out of Maori tracks.
On hearing this alarming news, the Governor’s
mind was at once made up. He gave strict
directions to the household that the settler’s
arrival was to be kept quite secret, and the man
himself was requested to keep closely to his room
in the Governor’s house for a day or two.
Soldiers were drafted so quietly and in such small
detachments on board the Driver before daybreak
that no Maori, and not even a European settler,
had any idea of what was going on.
After breakfast Captain Grey, unaccompanied,
sauntered leisurely to the jetty, got into a boat,
and was pulled off to the war vessel, which steamed
slowly past Porirua to the northward, until out of
sight of the land.
During the night, however, the vessel was
turned back, and before daylight on the 23rd July,
the forces, 128 soldiers and 38 blue-jackets were
landed in the Driver’s boats at the foot of
Rauparaha’s pah.
The surprise was complete, the force over-
powering, but the old chief made a desperate
struggle to escape, and would not give in until
assured that no injury would be done to him.
He was then at once transferred to the
Driver and subsequently held as a State prisoner
on H.M.S. Calliope in Wellington.
There was some desultory fighting after this,
but it was spiritless, for the capture of their
greatest strategist was a blow from which the
Maoris did not recover for many years.
Had the combination of the tribes been effected,
there was no force in New Zealand then that
could have withstood them, and the Wellington
settlement would have been wiped out.
But this is how even the latest “ History of
New Zealand,” written by Mr. F. J. Moss, late
M.H.R., and now British Resident at Raro-
tonga, passes judgment on Governor Grey’s dash-
ing exploit: “This seizure on suspicion was one
of those deeds which may possibly be excused ’
as precautionary ; but such deeds undoubtedly
create suspicion, deepen mistrust, and often sow
the seeds of future mischief. That which is not
straightforward is seldom advantageous in the
end.”
# * *
The Governor had settled the Maori diffi-
culty, but he had now a more desperate war to
wage with the land-sharks, chief among whom
were the missionaries. What between their
Bible and jew’s-harp land-exploitations, and
illegal purchases of enormous blocks under the
penny-an-acre and ten-shilling-an-acre proclama-
tions of Governor Fitzroy, which had been issued
without due authority, all the good available
land existing within reasonable distance of
.Auckland had been bought by private persons
for next to nothing, and they were asking such
exorbitant prices for it that extension of settle-
ment —the great need of the colony became
impossible.
To give a few instances of missionary greed,
selected from many others, Rev. R. Taylor claimed
50,000 acres ; Rev. Henry Williams, 22,000 ; Mr.
George Clark, Church Missionary Agent, 10,000;
Mr. W. T. Fairburn, C.M.A., 20,000; Mr. James
Kemp, C.M.A., 18,552, etc., etc. The Rev.
Henry Williams, indeed, had very enlarged views
on missionary occupation of land, for in December,
1835, he forwarded “a deed of trust of land,
belonging to natives,” to Sir Richard Bourke
the Governor of New South Wales (New Zealand
was then an appanage of that colony), and to the
secretary of the Church Missionary Society in
London, with a request that the missionaries at
the Bay of Islands might be appointed trustees
for all the lands of hew Zealand, in order to
“preserve it from the intrigues of designing
men!”
As may be imagined, neither Governor Bourke
nor the secretary of the C.M.S. could be induced
to grant this modest request.
Under the* law of the colony, as it then stood,
no person could purchase land in excess of 2560
acres. It is generally stated that Sir George
Grey of his own motion proceeded to make void
all purchases exceeding this limit, but actually
he did nothing of the kind. He selected one
“THE ILLS THAT FLESH IS HEIR TO.”

Haven’t seen him about lately.”
GEORGE: “Well, sir, he’s laid up, poor chap, with what they calls Haricot Veins!”
purchase as a test case and had it tried in the
Supreme Court. There it was hardly fought,
thoroughly argued out, and a very elaborate
judgment given against the grantees. The result
was the cancellation of the grants in cases where
they exceeded the limit allowed by law, the issue
of grants for 2256 acres only, and the accession of
much land in the neighbourhood of Auckland to
the Government, thus allowing room for the ex-
pansion of settlement.
But what a storm raged round the devoted
head of Captain Grey ! In a despatch to the
Secretary of State for the Colonies, on 20th June,
1846, he stated what he was about to do, and
added that a great outcry and much opposition
might be anticipated from the powerful interests
involved ; that all the legislative councillors,
Government officer’s, and missionaries would join
his opnonents; and that these were the classes
who urged that the natives should be allowed to
do what they liked with their land.
He was right, there was an outcry —a howl of
disappointed greed and sordid avarice.
But in this struggle the Governor had one firm
supporter, and he, though a churchman and a
bishop, one gs truly a man as the Governor him-
self, to wit, Bishop Selwyn, some of whose letters
on this and kindred subjects the missionary party
endeavour to keep wholly out of sight. They are
certainly never quoted nor referred to.
In 1843 Bishop Selwyn informed the Church
Missionary Society by letter that the land pur-
chases of the missionaries had “an injurious
effect upon the minds of the natives and the Eng-
lish settlers.”
In. 1847 the Bishop wrote to the clergy of his
diocese that the land purchases of the missionaries
had created jealousies, affected the character of
the society, and alienated Maori affections. “All
this,” he adds, “I will undertake to prove if it
should ever be necessary; but I earnestly desire
to be spared the painful duty by your quiet
acquiescence in the Governor’s proposal.”
But the Rev. Henry Williams refused to make
a quiet surrender of his land ; so the Church Mis-
sionary Society, for its own credit’s sake, com-
pelled to make an example, removed his name
from the list of their missionaries.
After a few years he was reinstated, at (this
circumstance, we believe, has never been made
public) the earnest entreaty of Sir George Grey.

After the capture of Rauparaha and during
the final operations against Rangihaeata, nine
prisoners were brought in to Porirua by the troops.
They were immediately tried by court-martial;
one was declared insane, seven sentenced to be
transported to Van Dieuian’s Land, and the
ninth, a young chief Martin Luther Wareaita to
be hanged as a rebel for bearing arms against the
imperud forces, though there was not even a
suspicion of murder against him. The Governor
was in another part of the colony and heard

The Governor wrote ordering that the mur
the Governor wrote ordering that the mur
derers, who had at once been seized by the
natives themselves and handed over to Captain
Laye, the officer in command of the troops at

But Captain Laye had meanwhile convened a

Van Dieman s Land.
An animated corresnondence with the Home
authorities ensued. The Governor insisted on
the jurisdiction of the Civil powers, while the War
Office insisted on martial rights.
No direct satisfaction was ever afforded to Cap-
tain Grey in the matter, but on his return to
England he found that instructions had been
issued that in future all criminals taken by the
troops were to be handed over to the Civil power.
In this connection it may not be out of place to
give Sir George Grey’s views on the subject of
death-sentences. Asa general rule he is of
opinion that the worst use you can put a man to
is to hang him.
A few months back there was great excitement
over the death-sentence bejng passed on two mere
boys in England for the murder of their father, a
man who had brutally ill-treated his wife—their
mother.
Through an error in the cablegram, the New
Zealand papers stated that both lads had been
executed, and this was generally believed, though
one was respited at the last moment.
Sir George was much agitated when he read
the cablegram, and talking over the matter,
referred also to an execution of lads that had
taken place in N.S. Wales.
He declared that, so far from a beneficial result
having been obtained by holding executions in
private, the public sense had been deadened, and
people were no longer alive to the horror of what
took place, and the cruel abuse of the law which
might result. Public feeling was, in other
respects, now being educated to a better tone,
and though in past generations, when the public
mind was to a perhaps larger extent brutalised,
lads were often executed, such a thing could not
take place in public nowadays without exciting
a thrill of horror, that would sweep away all such
abominations.
“If,” said he, “an attempt had been made to
carry out the sentence on those boys in the full
light of day, I believe public feeling would have
been strong enough to prevent the executions.”
# * #
The next great exercise of the Governor’s
power for a time embroiled him with the whole
population, and he was widely reviled for what
was afterwards acknowledged to be the most
beneficial act of his career.
An Act was passed by the Imperial Parliament
in 184(3 granting a Constitution to New Zealand.
But the suggestors, and to some extent the authors,
of this Constitution were the wealthy squatters of
the colony and their powerful friends among the
English aristocracy. The suffrage granted was so
very limited that all power would fall into the
hands of the land-grabbers, and considering the
small number of Europeans and their great greed
for Maori lands, the Governor did not think it
safe to trust the destinies of the natives to the
settlers.
Captain Grey therefore declined to put the
Constitution in operation, and adduced such solid
reasons that the Home Government gave way and
suspended the charter for five years.
Popular indignation ran high throughout the
colony, meetings were held in all centres of popu-
lation, settlers’ constitutional associations sprang
up everywhere, petitions couched in the strongest
and most uncomplimentary terms were showered
on the Colonial Office and Parliament; and, from
one end of New Zealand to the other, the Gover-
nor was the most unpopular and best abused man
to be found in it.
Meanwhile, Captain Grey cared for none of these
things, but quietly drafted a Constitution on
lines he deemed best suited for a colony that
should in time become a democratic nation. It
was finally adopted after a few alterations which
were in no case improvements. But, notwith-
standing these amendments, when, in 1852, it
finally passed the Imperial Parliament, New Zea-
land possessed what was acknowledged to be the
most workable Constitution, as well as the most
bberal, possessed by any British colony at that
time.
# # #
, With the Canterbury Association there
was a revival of the fight against land monopoly.
There a high price was placed on land with the
avowed intention of preventing labourers from
becoming land-owners, and so keeping them in a
state of dependence and creating a pseudo-aris-
tocracy. Sir George Grey—for he had been
knighted in 1848—again entered the lists against
the squatters, and issued a proclamation reducing
the price of land in the provincial district, outside
the company’s first purchase, to 10s. an acre. He
also devised means for preventing the establish-
ment of a State Church, which was contemplated
by the company.
In the latter he was successful, but in the
former he was eventually defeated by an unholy
compact, or understanding, between the squatters
and the officials of the Band Office, and the inven-
tion of “ gridironing,” which his departure from
the colony soon after rendered him unable to
defeat.
* * *
Among other good services, the credit of
which have been denied to Sir George Grey, was
the establishment of the liberal constitution of the
Church of England, in New Zealand, making it
a free and independent body, with none of the
evils of state church ism.
This constitution is always attributed to Bishop
Selwyn, the Bishop’s denial notwithstanding.
In 1867, when on the point of leaving for Eng-
land, Bishop Selwyn delivered a farewell address
at Auckland in which occurred the following
passage:—
“ I would now say a few words with regard to
the Synod and the Constitution. The speakers
who have referred to that subject have mentioned
me as the originator of that Constitution. I have
now the pleasure of telling you that there was
something more touching in the origin of that
Constitution than most persons are aware of. The
first draft of the present Constitution was drawn
by Sir George Grey on a sick bed at Taranaki (in
1850), and it was the fruit of those feelings which
come upon the mind in sickness—-when a man sets
aside thoughts of Government and the cares of
this world, am and knows, as a Christian man, that
he has something better to think of than the
perishable things of this life.
Patsy O’Connor, brother of the Jaynial
Dan, N.S.W, Postmaster-General, has "been
appointed C.P.S. at Bulli. Patsy’s favourite ex-
pression is “ Inferentially so.” He has read in a
barrister’s chambers and spent some years in an
attorney’s office and is credited with having
occasionally edited in advance the spontaneous
post-prandial orations of his famous brother. He
should therefore be capable of supplying the rural
Dogberrys with good law and sonorous modes of
expressing themselves.
Extracts from the diary of Jameson ; one
of Butcher and Bandit Stanley’s crowd :—
April 7th. — Marched to Vombo, quite the quickest
march we have done, owing to a good level road, and Mr.
Stanley doing rear-guard with some of his Somalis him-
self. How he did lay his stick about the lazy ones, and
the Somalis whacked away too. It was a sight for sore
eyes to see the lame, the sick, the halt, and the blind
running with their loads, as if they were feathers ; and I
was delighted to see some of my men catch it hot, after
1 had been told by Mr. Stanley himself not to strike them.
The march was otherwise uninteresting.
Editor Bulletin. —Sir, —In a recent issue
of The Bulletin a paragraph states : “ It takes
£5 10s. to make a ton of Queensland sugar. This
includes labour, interest, manufacturing—every-
thing.” Would that it were so'! The following,
however, is dry, hard factLowest price ac-
cepted for cane on field, 8s. per ton ; lowest cost
of cutting and loading, 2s. 6d. per ton ; lowest cost
of hauling to mill, 2s. 6d. per ton ; or 13s. per ton
delivered at mill. The lowest computation (ten
tons of cane to one ton of sugar) gives us £6 10s.
Prom the rollers, through clarifiers, subsiders,
triple-effect, vacuum pan, coolers, centrifugals,
drying and bagging ; with cost of lime, sulphur,
superphosphate, and bags another £6 10s. must be
added, giving a total of £13. It is the boast of a
now prominent Queensland M.L.A. that he can
produce a ton of sugar for £10; but the fact
remains that during several years of management
be never did so.—Yours truly, Sugar. Herbert
River, 23rd Jan.

Premier Munro, of Victoria, strongly
suspects Booth’s “Darkest England” scheme.
At the beginning of the century a million
yearly was added to the population of France.
Now the increase has sunk to 937,000.
Premier Griffith has taken a new departure
this year by enrolling a number of working men
on the Queensland list of Justices of the Peace.
The prospect of sharing in the proposed
titles should bring Sir Patrick Jennings back
from the Darling Downs to public life in N.S.W.
Says Gladstone, in allusion to Home
Rule :—“I have been rolling this stone up the hill
for five years, and now it has rolled down again,
and I am eighty-one.”
A German cobbler has been sentenced to
two months for /esc majeste because he refused to
rise when someone in a fcabaret shouted “hoch!”
for the Emperor with the sore ears.
Ex-Attorney General Wrixon will not
resign his seat at the Federal convention in favour
of present Attorney-General Shiels. You see,
this is Wrixon’s last chance for a title.
“ The young men,” said Squatter Abbott
in his evidence before the N.S.W. Labour Com-
mission, “ are unionists, while the old men are
not. ” The clock of the world moves on.
The good old 1.0. U. policy has now been
fairly initiated in West Australia. Premier
Forrest’s first loan is a modest one, and only
reaches a little more than a million and a third.
Champion compares the Australian strikers
to “an army of lions led by asses.” Better to be
misled by an honest ass than to be purposely de-
ceived by a designing fox of the Champion variety,
A certain N.S. Wales M.L.A. has sent a
bill in to Government at 3s. per head for the
board and lodging of the police troopers who
were provided for his protection during the recent
strike.
The N.S.W. Foreign-trade party up to
lately used to drag the Single-tax across the
trail of the fiscal issue. Now they try to divert
attention to Co-operation as the panacea for social
and commercial ills.
At the next general election in Queens-
land it is understood that MTlwraith, now repre-
senting North Brisbane with Premier Griffith,
who is thus his colleague in a twin sense, will
stand for the Burrum.
South Australia will shortly go to the
London market for another loan, this time for
£1,532,913. If this loan be floated, the S.A.
public debt will be £22,684,000, or over £7O per
head of the population.
Jack Gardiner, “ the football member ”
for Carlton (Melb.), considers that the Federation
of the English-speaking people of the world will
shortly be consummated. Which just about
decides the question in the negative.
Does anyone know the real reason why
Freetrade Secretary Pulsford resigned ? Wasn’t
it for the very same reason that Mr. Henry
Tennant Donaldson is now honorary secretary
to the political committee of the N.S.W.
National Club ?
Goschen put his finger on the blot, speak-
ing last week at Leeds. He complained of the
Australian tariffs as hostile to England, and
rightly argued that they proved the sympathy
with Britain to be very thin. In fact, we are
practically cut off already.
The British Crown is not entailed on the
Wettins. Even should “Tummy” live long
enough—that’s doubtful —the House of Commons
may refuse to uphold his claim. Now, we’ve lost
our J.P.-ship, we feel inclined to have a go for
the sceptre and shekels.
Gladstone tells Australian Labour Delegate
Fitzgerald that he does not believe in labour
legislation which, he says, tends to destroy
individualism among the workers. Gladstone,
like his great Australian prototype, Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan, is not a socialist.
Ex-Premier Dibbs said in the presence of
Governor Jersey, the other day, that nothing
would come of Imperial Federation, but that
“ eventually Australia would become an Empire
in itself.” What does Dibbs mean ? Those who
mean Republic, say Republic.
“ When once Australian Federation is
completed,” says Lord Carington, “England will
have an easy task in influencing the Australian
national feeling in her favour.” Let this signifi-
cant but, perhaps, unintentional warning be not
neglected by Australian nationalists.
An agitation for the adoption of one
common military uniform throughout the Aus-
tralian defence forces is cropping up. Before
devoting so much attention to uniformity of cloth-
ing it would be as well for young Australia to seek
Uniformity of justice, social responsibilities, and
material comforts.
A threepenny journal, with death on its
little brow, has budded forth in a Sydney suburb
under the ambitious title of the Australian.
It’s a monthly, and so may not droop and die till
autumn. Its supplement is entitled The Aus-
tralian Federalist. Is this the journal so long
threatened by B. R. Wise?
Sir George Grey favours “ a loose kind of
federation” of the English-speaking peoples in
preference to a hard-and-fast business bargain.
The same sort of federation, in fact, which a
sensible man generally has with his mother-in-
law. Where interests are hopelessly diverse
federation cannot be too loose.
The Lang statue may help to start the
young Australian reading his book on Freedom
and Independence for Australia—with Lord
Jersey’s approbation. There has been a long line
of monarchs since Dr. Lang urged the Republic—
your Belmores, Loftuses, Caringtons, and what
not, all taking the same profound interest as Lord
Jersey.
“Nigger-sugar” Cowley, who, imme-
diately after delivering a passionately vindictive
attack on Griffith, then leader of the Queensland
Opposition, accepted a portfolio from that
potentate, has publicly explained that he “ came
down from the North an ardent Separationist,
and would go back an ardent Federationist.”
Same old “ thirty pieces of silver.”
The Maitland Mercury “fully believes
that the ranks of free-labeurers include some good
men in every respect. ’ The Bulletin doesn’t.
It believes that “free ' labourers are good men in
no respect whatever. The man who, for the sake
of a temporary easing of his own position, is
willing to betray his brethren into white slavery,
has plumbed about as deep an abyss of degrada-
tion as is possible to a creature walking upright
on two legs and possessing no tail.

MR. DECYNIC : “ Oh, yes, it was indeed a howling success.”
“ The M‘Kinley tariff has had the effect of
lowering wages in America, and the working men
are expected to protest against its continued
operation,” says the cableman. Now, this is and
must be a mere expression of opinion, but the
opinion is shamelessly put forward to poison the
Australian public mind in favour of the British
exporters. The M‘Kinley tariff will pull down
wages in Canada.
The S.A. Parliament passed an act last
session providing for a system of voting quite new
to Australia, voters who may be absent from
their district on the day of election can, under
the new system, vote through the post-office, the
vote being signed in the presence of the post-
master, on a form supplied by the returning-
officer. The system is to be tried shortly in the
bye-election for Wooroora.
Look, now that Bradlaugh is dead>
at the heroic fight made by him to assert
the right of unbelievers to sit in the Mouse
of Commons. It lasted five years, 1880 to
1885, but, alas ! it broke his health, and ruined
his pocket, so that not long Mck he declared he
would have to retire, solely from poverty—as
Burns has said he, too, must give in. The collapse
of the Opposition at last was absurd, but it did
not take place until men like Northcote and
Wolff had made their capital by trading on
bigotry. What a satire on “British fair play”
and the mutability of? human opinion is the fact
that the resolution deriving the right of Brad-
laugh, the atheist, to take the oath has now been
unanimously expunged from the records of the
Commons 1

Mr. J. J. Lee, chief clerk in charge of the
criminal branch of the N.S.W. Crown Solicitor’s
Office, was first appointed on 15th February, 1845.
His service has been continuous ever since, making
a term of 45 years.
The oldest English clergyman is aged one
hundred, viz.. Rev. Mr. Elliot, of a parish called
Randwick, which he has stuck to. in nernetual
curacy ever since the year before Queen Victoria
was born —73 years.

Two—husband and wife —to Royalty; one to
science and intrepidity—Cook; one to a dead
Governor—Bourke; one to Republican Lang ; and
one to “ Morality ” Mort.
Two Dromios, or two Antipholi—Gov.
Jarsey and Railway Minister Shiels, of Melbourne;
only one of them is ever so much handsomer
than the other. The handsomer is, of course, the
man who has the bluer blood.

Minister of Education Bews (S.A.) says
he was never so happy in all his life as when he
was a lad and herded pigs at half-a-crown a week.
De gustibus non est disputandum. What a noble
ambition to put before schoolboys !
Rev. George Walters, of Sydney, says
that he once tried to deliver an amusing address,
but “nobody laughed,” and adds: “How I
escaped a lunatic asylum is still a mystery to me. ”
How all the humorless parsons escape the lunatic
asylums is a mystery to us.

Mr. George Walton, the gifted young
English artist, whose portrait, “My Mother,”
will long be remembered by those who saw it at
Sydney Art Society’s exhibition, is dead—of con-
sumption. During his stay in Australia Mr.
Walton resided chiefly in Melbourne.
Good old Dr. Singleton, of Melbourne, has
retired for ever from his philanthropic work—one
of the true salt of the earth. For sixty years he
has walked about in a shabby coat visiting the
sick, feeding the hungry, consoling the afflicted—
and being imposed upon by the loafers.

based, be it said, on a fine imagination and a
unique power of laying on three colours in three
words—is making any number of literary enemies
for that young Anglo-Indian. We find the envious
Sims clumsily scoffing at a recent story by “ Back-
yard Tippling.” s
E. N. Newbury, the S.A. official who
vanished at the same time as his cash, preached—
a fortnight before his little trouble —in a suburban
Anglican Church. Whittington, a leading audit
official and the man who discovered the deficiency,
is a leading light in the congregation, and was one
of the worshippers.
Bishop Julius, of Christchurch, Maori -
land, has shocked some of his congregation. He
was proceeding to the cathedral on a Sunday
morning with a sermon in his pocket, when he
found himself -behind time. There was no cab in
sight, but a friendly fish-cart being at hand, the
Bishop jumped in and was in his pulpit “on
time.”
His Honor District Court Judge Wilkin-
son has entered into an arrangement with the
Queensland Government for an edition of his
excellent “Australian Magistrate.” The reward
is a very considerable sum, and the work engages
the Judge’s leisure and vacation. Verily, no man
is a prophet in his own province, though he may
be deemed a legal authority in a neighbouring
one.
The pleasantly-remembered Mr. James
Sheen Dowling (ex-District Court Judge, and
sometime N.S.W. Attorney-General) is about to
give to the world “ The Note-book of an Austra-
lian Judge.” The volume will be issued from the
Sydney press. If the ex-Judge will only tell the
beat stories about that other Judge who used to
play II Penseroso to his L’Allegro, the book
should be very amusing indeed.
Alfred Cellier, the composer, who returned
to Australia by the Oroya, looks extremely ill, we
are soi'ry to say. He cherishes fond memories of
curry partaken of at Colombo, and intends return-
ing by that route, so as to again delight his palate.
Alfred kept the Adelaide reporters —always
hungry themselves —for nearly a quarter of an
hour telling them about the curry, until they
became exasperated. Cellier leaves Australia next
May.
We notice that Mr. G. H. Supple, the
journalist, sentenced to death in Melbourne 20
years ago for shooting Walsh in his effort to shoot
G. P. Smith, is working in New Zealand. He
made a mark in literature as far back as_ 1856,
with a book on the Anglo-Norman Invasion of
Ireland, praised in warm terms by the London
literary papers. Barrister Fisher suggested the
•mint on which Chief-Justice Higinbotham let
him off.
When Miss Hannah de Rothschild was
married to Lord Rosebery, the whole of her dot,
one million sterling, was settled on the Earl, and
on the deatli of the Baroness Meyer de Roths-
child, Lady Rosebery’s mother, the entire pro-
perty in England of her late father passed into
her possession, and from hers into that of Lord
Rosebery, who does not possess the power, if he
wished to exercise it, of willing it away from the
children of his marriage. The amount of money,
stocks, and other negotiable securities which fell
to the share of Lady Rosebery was about
£4,000,000 sterling, and this was exclusive of real
property, pictures, plate, &c. Lord Rosebery
holds all this for life only.

Society
Among those who attended ex-Governor
Carington’s London lecture was James R. Fairfax,
of the S.M. Herald. James Fairfax must have
a nickel-steel cheek. He ought to have buried
himself in a cellar upon such an occasion. It was
his cold-blooded and cowardly paper which, after
justifying the brutal and unmanly action of the
law in regard to the Mount Rennie boys, turned
round on the morning upon which they were, as
it well knew, to be ruthlessly slaughtered, and
said that they had not had a fair trial. Anyone
who doubts this statement can easily verify it by
reading the leading article in the S.M. Herald of
7th January four years ago.
* # &
Governor Jersey should make himself
fully acquainted with the Mount Rennie affair, so
that he may on all occasions know what to do
with the prerogative of his royal mistress. Any
curiosity which Lord Jersey may already feel in
the matter should be stimulated by the tact that
he last week visited Darlinghurst Gaol and stood
upon the very gallows whereon the reputation of
Baron Carington as a man of discernment and a
lover of equal justice to all citizens was hanged
by the neck until it was dead.
* * *
The Baron, after diagnosing the Austra-
lian body politic for five years, has written a
prescription. It consists of but one word —Titles.
Bebauble the governing classes in effect, says
Carington, and they will keep the voters in order.
Carington knows that the lion in the path of the
Australian democracy is a lioness. The Austra-
lian politician who could, if left to himself, resist
the offer of a title is unable to resist the impor-
tunities of his wife, who always wants to be a
Lady and splash mud with her carriage-wheels
into the face of those cats her female enemies.
The Bulletin knows one man, who under pressure
from his wife abandoned thousands of pounds
annual income in order that she might be “ My
Lady.” The retention of the present system of
conferring Imperial titles, which system is
gradually but surely extending in the hereditary
direction (Clarke and Cooper to wit) means tho
perpetuation of a poisonous corruption. Not
even the existence of Lady , with her big
crop of illegitimate brats, can assuage the Aus-
tralian feminine thirst for ennoblement.
# # #
Baron Carington’s lecture last week serves
to remind us that Wentworth had some mis-
chievous ideas—one, that there ought to be an
Australian peerage. And with such a bauble
dangling, before his deluded fancy, he refused a
baronetcy.
# * *
Carington advises the institution of purely
colonial orders of knighthood, &c. But that
would not do the trick. Nothing is yet fashion-
able here which has not been imported from
abroad. The Australian woman with social and
knightly aspirations does not live in Australia.
She lives in the green fields far off—among the
dukes and nobles of Hingland, and the gilded
villains of the London Journal.
* * *
There is more humour in Jersey than you
might think, if you only judge him by looking at
him. He opened a church-bazaar at Goulburn
the other day, and in the course of his speech
said : —“lt gives pleasure at all times, and I think
it would to any of us, to assist in any good Chris-
tian work. ” Song-and-dance—hoo :
The recent Carington ought to read
yet another paper on “ Australia as I saw
it.” The chief heads of the discourse to
be a certain large and successful massacre; a
burnt-down drapery warehouse —the one whose
proprietor Carington sympathised with; the
awful language used by that cabman whom
Carington found cursing at his fare by
night on the memorable occasion when he paid
the shilling; the bashed-up footballer whom he
carted home in his barouche; the great episode at
Bourke when the beloved Gov. took truck-shunt-
ing exercise; the woman who invited him to
attend a wedding breakfast in a baby-linen shop;
the awful, beastly way the locusts flew into his
mouth and silted him up every time he yawned •
and one or two minor items.
* * *
After reading the address of welcome pre-
sented to Governor Jersey by the members of the
Goulburn (N.S.W.) Presbytery, we are of opinion
that the Presbytery, when composing it, were not
quite sure whether they were addressing Lord
Jersey or the Lord Almighty.
* # *
The Bulletin regards “pink-eye” as a
graceful compliment to Earl Jersey. Its advent
was simultaneous with that of His Excellency,
who, had we been a heathenish and superstitious
people, would have been slain at once on the
evidence against him.
* * *
The Wilson murder case has set talking
the Melbourne gossips, the lower-middle, old and
young tabbies, more than anything that has hap-
pened for a long time. Melbourne has had three
murders in a week, and all by amateurs. Wilson,
a tram-conductor, slaughtered his sweetheart like
a sheep, with a razor, in Darling Gardens, Colling-
woqd, on Sunday night, at half-past eleven, while
sitting on a gas-pipe. There had been mischief-
making, her wise women friends persuading her to
take a decorator, with twice the wages of the
tram-conductor. She loved the assassin, but liked
the decorator’s money. The mischievous bour-
geois idea as to Work being Disgraceful is at the
basis of nearly all —Respectability.

The following from the Australian Star
(Sydney) is too rich to need comment: —
THE MURDER WAVE
BLOODSHED IN VICTORIA.
THE SWOOP OF THE TOMAHAWK AND THE BASH OF THE
BLUDGEON—POISON MIXTURES AND PISTOL SHOTS—THE
FLASH OP THE RAZOR—JEALOUSY AND SLAUGHTER —
THE LOVER’S CURE —A GRIM RECORD.
(FROM OUR MELBOURNE REPRESENTATIVE.)
There is a boom in bloodshed in Victoria, and the citizen
and bushman are simultaneously shivering in their shoes,
and keeping their eyes open for the destructive assassin.
A wave of death by violence is spreading over the land,
and the police are powerless to break its flow. People
hitherto regarded as innocent and law-abiding citizens
have called in the assistance of the tomahawk, the razor,
the pistol, the poison cup and the bludgeon to slay their
fellows. It is a sad and terrible condition of society, and
the possibilities are that it will become worse by-and-bye.
This is a horrible subject to deal with, but the duty must
not be shirked.
Lower down, this great literary artist proceeds to
show how
“Across Stella Marks’ throat was a greatgash, from which
the rich blood of a robust girl gurgled forth.”
n * #
There were no fresh murdered corpses
lying about in Melbourne at time of going to
press.
* * *
Miss “Cricket” Watson declares that,
despite the Supreme Court decision, she will wed
Mr. Kitz. She intends, meanwhile, putting her
case before the Privy Council.
* * *
A young and sprightly Victorian widow
of some twenty-five summers, left by her first
husband with a fortune of some thousands a year,
was not long in forgetting her bereavement and
seeking the protection of a second swain. She
first engaged herself to a certain middle-aged
gallant naval captain, but soon transferred her
affections to a young scion of a well-respected
Australian family, holding a minor N.S.W.
Civil Service positon. 22nd January was fixed for
the nuptials, the reverend pastor of the fashionable
fane of St. John’s, Darlinghurst, was brought
from the Blue Mountains to perform the ceremony,
and a sumptuous breakfast was laid in a marquee
on the lawn of a friend of both parties. St. John's
was beautifully decorated, and no expense
was spared to make the event unusually
rekerky. The church was filled to overflowing
with the beauty and fashion of Darlinghust to
witness the event; but after waiting for over an
hour, neither bride nor bridegroom put in an
appearance. Some said the bride had eloped with
her first lover; some said the bridegroom had a
big head on him after the dinner at which he had
taken leave of bachelorhood on the previous
evening. However, in the end it turned out that
at the dinner aforesaid the bridegroom had
divulged, in a moment of extreme joyfulness,
that he was already married to the lady, and that
the honeymoon was over; however, he didn’t
mind having a second. Hence the confusion that
reigned at Darlinghurst on the following day.
Thus happily ended the practical joke into which
the young couple had drifted by reason of their
unwillingness to disclose their private marriage.
They journeyed overland to Adelaide, and left
for England by the Carthage.

Wagga must be a nice place to live in,
especially for “young gentlewomen.” From last
Saturday’s Sydney Daily Telegraph :
VyANTED, for Private Family, quiet, sensible
’ ' young Gentlewoman ; capable taking full charge
young children, and as companion and help to lady of
house. Salary £2O, with bonus at end of year. Apply
by letter, Home Comfort, box 27, Wagga Wagga.
“ Young gentlewomen ” must be at a heavy dis-
count in N.S. Wales when they are expected to
rush a billet of nursery-maid and nursery gover-
ness, cook and housemaid, laundress and soullery-
mai'd, with perhaps such little additional posts
thrown in as dressmaker and private secretary to
the “lady ”of the house, and pianoforte accom-
panist to some screaming member of the family,
for the munificent emolument of 7s. B|d., plus bed
and tucker, every Saturday, and the prospect of
a possible bonus of perhaps a whole week’s wages
at Christmas.

It has been computed that “cruelty” is
the allegation- as against “adultery”—in nine out
of twelve divorce-suits. “Lack of maintenance”
ranks next as an aggravation. Who shall say that
her bodily comfort isn’t lovely woman’s tenderest
corn?

plains of a loss of upwards of £30,000 during the

# * »
Yatala labour prison, the S.A. convict
prison, is famed for its Sunday services and the
excellence of its choir. About half its inmates
have either ornamented the Adelaide choirs or
thumped pulpits in the biblical city.
# # *
Here are some nice facts concerning a
well-known official who presides over an Aus-
tralian Court. His Honor is up to his eyes in
debt, and his dishonoured paper is as common in
loan-offices and solicitors’ hands as are flowers in
spring. It is no uncommon circumstance for him
to be “bailed up” in the street on his way to
Court by mondaines and toughs—in whose power
he is alleged to be —and made to empty out his
pockets; and it is further stated that persons,
with whom his Honor has had pecuniary trans-
actions, have a penchant for bringing cases in
his Court.
* * *
The cableman told us (and the newspapers
still tell us) that Boehm, the sculptor, was found
dead in his studio by Princess Louise—who, on
entering unannounced, was shocked to find him
prone on the floor. As a matter of fact, the
curiously Bohemian Princess had been in the
studio conversing with the artist, who is said
to have entered an ante-chamber, and while there
the Princess heard a noise, and, on entering,
found Sir Edgar dead. Yet there was no inquest.
The case prima jacie recalls that of Jimmy Morri-
son, the dapper Melbourne attorney, who was
found dead in his office by a lady friend of his. In
that case, however, there was an inquest, as
Morrison’s friend was a governess, not a Princess.

A Sydney amateur photographer has been
stopped from taking sun-pictures in Melbourne
Public Gardens. Hard lines this, patenting
Nature.

Yes, I’m Jarsey on the travel,
For I never duties shirk,
And my meek heart cannot cavil
Or at Christian or at Turk ;
Still I’ll own to, on this gravel,
Predilection for the Kirk ;
And, with parsons on the snavel—
It’s a good old Christian work.
For the gambler there’s the “ scaffle,”
For his ways are ways of murk ;
Try the curb, when fails the snaffle,
Much of wickedness we burke,
Though we cannot hope to baffle
All the betting-men who lurk;
But when parsons run a raffle —
It’s a good old Christian work.
HIS SALARY WAS BETTER.
Poor Stella Marks is dead and gone,
Yes, gone away to Glory,
And in a melancholy tone
We sing her cruel story :
She was engaged to one who talked
Of forging Hymen’s fetter,
Yet with another out she walked—
His salary was better.
She dearly loved her proper swain,
’Twas joy to have him hug her,
But still she could not quite refrain
From thinking of thfe “shugger”;
E’en as she sat on Johnny’s lap,
The while he used to pet her,
She’d not forget that other chap
Whose salary was better.
Each time her sweetheart raised a row
His jealousy she’d stifle,
And bid him not to trouble now
About a foolish trifle ;
She’d see the “ other ” nevermore—
But shortly Johnny met her
A-strolling with his rival, for
His salary was better.
Then, almost daft with jealous rage,
Did Johnny seek his Stella —
Just like that darkie on the stage
Whom dudelings call Othellab.
He loved her so—half-crazy lad !
He felt he could have c[n)t her —
She never would have fooled him had
His salary been better.
And Johnny begged her to explain,
We fear she couldn’t do it:
He took her by the throat, ’tis plain,
And put a razor through it.
The hot blood spurted from the gash,
She spoke no word or letter,
But died. Why did she want a mash
Whose salary was better ?
Then Johnny went to the perleece;
Those active men and zealous
Arrested him, and he’s at peace—
At least he isn’t jealous ;
He mourns the pretty damsel dead,
For how can he forget her ?
The rival suitor’s eyes are red
Whose salary was better.
Poor Stella Marks ! Poor Stella Marks !
She’s gone away to Glory :
An end has come to all her larks,
A sudden end and gory.
Before all girls, to give them pause,
For warning we must set her—
Don’t have a “ second string ” because
His salary is better.

Mrs. Cohen, the Adelaide Mayoress, is a
fairly good public speaker. The lady is president
of the” “ Wattle Blossom League, run by the
local Natives, and frequently orates.
* * *
Gov. Kintore wanted Goyder, the S.A.
Surveyor-General, to assist him in his threatened
journey across the continent. Goyder said he
would rather be excused, and managed to back
out of it The great difficulty Kintore is trying
to get over is the inability to secure an experi-
enced official, and it is only obstinacy am, fear of
being badgered that prevents him throwing the
whole business overboard. Without an experi-
enced guide, the amateur party will get out ot
their track and finish their picnic somewhere
about Ballarat or Perth instead of Adelaide.

Frederick Woods, a Melbourne tradesman
convicted of stealing sundry bottles of scent from
a chemist’s shop, has been discharged without an
odor on his character. In the first place, there
wasn’t the slightest reason why I redenck should
pilfer perfume, except that it come a good deal
cheaper that way. In the second place, a number
of J.P.’s and mayors testified to prisoner s perfect
respectability. In the third place, a doctor and a
merchant explained that Frederick was a
martyr to his liver,” and therefore unnaccountable
for his conduct in seeking to smell nice on easy
terms; —
Any small amount of decency which
European royalty ever possessed seems to have
evaporated a good while ago. The last shred of it
vanished when that princely Italian, the Duke of
Aosta —the same spavined, scrofulous, morally-
busted potentate who was made King of Spain,
only to be thrown out by his disgusted subjects—
married his own niece with the sanction of the
Holy Church, and the imperial blessing of all
Europe. And now that Aosta has crumbled away,
md his desperately ugly bald head has been
bidden in the sepulchre of his fathers, the widowed
duchess proposes to marry the new duke, who
was first her cousin, and then her son by marriage,
Mid who is now to finish up by being her husband.
And then most likely she will die, and her second
ausband will run a harem of aunts and mothers-
m-law, or else he will elope with his grandmother
mder a special dispensation. The character of
Jleopatra begins to look up again in these days.
She only married her brother after all.
# * *
That “ enterprising Sydney coachman” who
colted a few weeks ago with his employer’s
daughter and heiress has not yet been heard of—
aor, for that matter, has the heiress. Incidents
of this sort are not very common in Australia as
yet, for the custom of hiring a garish menial with
portentous legs, and dressing him up in livery has
aot taken deep root in this country; but as the
gorged hanger-on increases and multiplies his
fiabit of borrowing the boss’s offspring will become
more and more marked. In America the custom
of eloping with the coachman is fixed and incur-
able, and even if that illiterate flunkey is an
blderly, ugly nigger it makes no difference;
practically it is the livery and not the live meat
he-slavey inside it that the girl falls in love with,
and if he were a Chinaman or a naval officer with
one leg he would probably get there all the same-
provided he had the clothes on. What ultimately
becomes of the girl and the gilded helot in such
cases is uncertain. Sometimes, if she is unusually
fortunate, her father may come after her and
pound her husband into a state of insensibility,
and kick him into next week, and then take her
back and keep her locked up on short rations q but
as a rule she is left to discover that he isn’t the
ethereal flunkey she took him for, and after that
she probably makes a precarious living for the
rest of her days in the washing industry. Any
man who insists on keeping a large, servile animal
in green clothes about the place has to take his
chance of finding his female descendant missing
some morning. The flunkey’s two chances in life
are to elope with an heiress or to start a public-
house, and naturally he slopes with the heiress
when he gets the show.

each front is supposed to indicate the wearer’s
predilection—the yachtsman displaying a minia-

Dear Bulletin,— Chinese cooks really are
the mouth-artists that you represent them to be.
I remember, years ago, in Melbourne, a Chinese
cook had a remarkable fame for the artistic man-
ner in wh'ch he used to serve up “ thin bread and
butter” with the ladies’ afternoon tea. The
butter was spread as smooth as a “ glassy sea,
and quite “fetched” the feminines and their
attendant dudes. One afternoon, when tea was
poured out, the bread and butter was’nt up to
time. Mine host himself went down to hurry up
Chow Wong. He found the latter standing in a
pensive attitude in front of the kitchen-range
with puffed-out cheeks, while on the table near
him were thin “ rounds ” of bread placed in single
fi'e, ready, apparently, for being buttered. Mine
Host stormed and raved at Chow Wong, but the
latter merely shook his head and rolled his eyes
in agony, while his mouth worked convulsively
with a sort of centrifugal wobble. Mine Host,
after several blasphemies, fell upon the heathen
with a kick- in the stern and an objurgation, when
—a jet of molten butter spirted promiscuously
over everything, and Chow Wong calmly replied :
“What foh? You spoilee buttee. _ ioo cold
to-day, buttee no spleadee. Lose time now ;
begin all oveh again.” — Yours, &0., Ihe Dipso-
maniac.
* * *
An English judge recently ruled that a
conversation between a husband and his
which it was sought to exclude as “private, was
“public,” because “both mothers-in-law were
present.”

* # *
A lady correspondent of the Pictorial
World enquires why women are less courageous
than men? The question is unnecessary, as there
is very little difference between the sexes in that
regard. A reliable authority on the subject has
settled the matter in a couple of sagacious lines ;
“Woman simulates designedly more fear than
she actually experiences ; man studiously conceals
a deal of that fear wherewith he is more or less
imbued.”
# * #
What is this we hear about a Government
department greasing its axles with “socials” to
all hands, given ostensibly by the bosses, but
really at the country’s expense ?
# * #
According to evidence adduced at a
recent inquest, a West Queensland squatter has
been in the habit of torturing his wife by all
styles of complicated ingenuity, from the common
“ knock-down blow ” to small doses of poison. He
rather fancied “tying her hands behind her back
and hanging her up by the feet for two hours.”
Another pastime was to force her down on her
back on a table and pretend to “screw her arms
out.” His wife was found dead on November 20
last with “ a graze on the nose and a black mark
across the throat.” Her loving husband has been
requested to give the usual official explanation.
# # #
The chief officers of the Sydney Board of
Health are understood to assert that if the Black
Death plague of the Middle Ages can possibly be
resuscitated by brutal filth, it will certainly re-
appear in Sydney. They tell ghastly stories of
sewage percolating through the kitchen floors of
small hotels, and of ordure bubbling into the baths
at the said fashionable hotels while lady-guests
are bathing. They go on to assert that the
plumbing-work necessary to place already-in-
spected premises in sanitary condition will cost
(or would, for the landlords, you may be sure, will
find a means of evading such absurd expense) a
million sterling. Meanwhile, Inspector Seymour
is having people fined heavily for offences against
the public health which display more grotesque
uncleanliness than could be conjured up by the
imagination of the champion dirty man.
#• # *
In Victoria the proportion of decrees nisi to
marriages celebrated is one-third per cent., and the
petitions for divorce half per cent., which means
that two out of every three applications prove
. successful. The-figures for England and Wales
show twice as large a proportion. _ In France
there is one divorce to every 77 marriages, and in
San Francisco one to every six ! Divorce is on
the increase throughout all civilised nations.
# * *
G. R Sims maintains that “kindness to
animals is in no way a sign of kindness to human
beings, or of natural goodness of disposition ”—a
theory also favoured by Wilkie Collins in his
creation of Count Fosco. But no writer seems to
have specially noted the indifference, if not dis-
like, to dumb animals that is invariably shown
by the “model housewife” type of female. She
looks askance at dogs, cats, or even pet birds, and
takes no personal interest in a horse unless it be
pulling her along. Then she is either fearful lest
the beast may bolt, or vexed with it for not
travelling faster. A particularly virtuous and
energetic matron has, moreover, a hearty con-
tempt for hen fowls, and cries aloud for their
assassination (to be followed by household con-
sumption) directly they go off the lay. She is
much more charitably disposed towards the old
rooster. Every woman is at heart a rake.

# # *
The N.S.W. Attorney-General's idea of
a reasonable Crown allowance for the defence of
a pauper prisoner indicted for a capital offence is,
it appears from the first case which has arisen
since the humane system of granting such allow,
ances was commenced under Mr. Simpson’s regime,
£l5 15s. for counsel and £lO 10s. for the attorney.
The amount applied for was 150 guineas for
counsel and 75 for the attorney ! The Bulletin
ventures to urge that, considering the amount of
the retainer which would have to be paid to a
senior counsel in the ordinary way, Mr. Simpson
might venture to stretch the Crown allowance a
little. If he did, nobody could fairly growl.

brush.

“Five persons poisoned with preserved
tongue.” But who keeps the record of all the

# * *
Drunkenness is punished in the Argentine
Republic by a sentence of eight days’ hard labour
on the public roads. This minimum penalty is
much more effective as a deterrent than the ss.
fine which rules in Australia.

Captain Anstruther Thomson, aide-de-
camp to Gov. Kintore, was married to Miss Clayre
■'-’ennant at Glenelg (S.A.) on Wednesday, 28th
Jan. The pair will probably leave for England
in a few weeks time to spend the honeymoon.
The wedding was a very pretty one, and was most
fashionably attended.
* * *
“We may be particular,” says a Mel-
bourne paper, “but we must confess that we do
not think the Sydney girls are as handsome as
those in Melbourne, unless all the good-looking
ones stop at home.” That is just it. The most
attractive Sydney girls do not eternally tramp the
Block, as is the custom down South.
* * *
A curious milliner’s advertisement appears
in a Melbourne paper this week : Miss So-and-So
announces herself as “ Late Mrs. So-and-So.” In
America, we dare say, you could hunt up a card
of Miss Solus, late Mrs. Double, formerly Mrs.
Tripler, previously Mrs. Quadd, and before that
Miss Solus, to which she has wearily returned.
Refreshing to be a Miss again—if you can. One
would like to see an old maid gammoning after
five divorces.
MARTYRS TO THEIR LIVERS.
Of the ills weak flesh is heir to
(As the medicos will swear to)
There is nothing so distracting
As a liver seldom acting ;
Let your home be ne’er so snuggish,
If that vital organ’s sluggish,
Why, you never can look cheerful
And your temper’s something fearful ;
You sit and eat your dinner
Like a miserable sinner,
Whilst the echo of your grumble
Is a deep eternal rumble,
And at intervals you shiver—
Wretched martyr to your liver!
’Tis the liver’s at the bottom
Of the horrors, when you’ve “ got ’em ; ”
If your spirits are affected,
And your countenance dejected,
’Tis the liver, never doubt it —
Yet one cannot do without it.
Should it happen to be active
Life is pleasant and attractive;
But, alas ! and lack-a-daisy !
When it drives a fellow crazy,
Thus impelling him to slaughter,
Or to jumping in the water.
All the corpses in our rivers
Have been martyrs to their livers.
Some men are to be pitied
When a crime they have committed ;
You can take your oath a gent, is
What we call non compos mentis
When there’s evidence revealing
That he has a turn for stealing.
Quite insane is his condition
If he fill a good position—
Though the Law will pass a curse on
Any seedy guilty person,
For opinion is emphatic
That such cannot be lunatic.
Thus a doctrine has arisen
Guarding wealthy folks from prison,
And our sympathy increases
For the holders of the “ pieces ”
When a well-connected Tottie
Does a swindle, she is “dotty,”
And the judges cry “ Forgive her—
Bretty martyr to her liver ! ”
With shirt-fronts hand-painted
We’re not yet acquainted,
But when they come quite into fashion
We shall spot at a glance,
At a dinner or dance,
Each gent’s own particular passion.
The fellow who hunts
Has dogs on his fronts,
And foxes and fences and ’osses ;
The parson who prays
Invites us to gaze
On miniature Heavens and Crosses,
The crank after cricket
Will show us a wicket.
Bat, ball, and a portrait of Giffen ;
Whilst the bookmaker tricky
Has “mugs” on his “dickey,”
Investing their “stuff ” on a “stiff-’un.”
The self-seeking mayor
A picture will wear
Of a knight by chain armour encumbered ;
And (some people assert)
On the land-boomer’s shirt
Will be “lots,” nicely coloured and numbered.
A youth who is tender
On one of the gender
That makes married-man such a martyr,
Will soon be beginnin’
To paint on his linen
The face of his inamorata.
But thousands of sinners,
At dances or dinners,
Who’ve pluck to put boldly before us
The things they love best,
Will display on the breast
A vision of legs—in the chorus.

The Referee.
.fohn Roberts recently scored 1100 points in six
consecutive breaks—spot barred, of course. If the
ordinary amateur, who “ fancies his game a little,” will
consider how many times he usually picks up his cue
in the course of playing a 50, and if he will consider that
an abnormal fear of “leaving anything on” would cause
the said number of times to he much greater than usual,
he can possibly form some faint idea of the start that
Roberts could comfortably give him in, say, a game of
2000 up. Take big George Bonnor as a representative
amateur, able to score with what looks like great
rapidity against an inferior opponent. Now, Roberts
would be able and very willing to give Bonnor 1845 out of
2000 points. And Bonnor, for his part, could give about
1400 in 2000 to heaps of “ decent ” players, so-called.
* *
An “open-air service” is held near the big
gymnasium at Darlinghurst, over which Larry Foley
presides. One evening an evangelist was telling the
crowd about “ turning the other side ” when a foe smites
you on the cheek. “ Wot rot!” said the practical Larry,
“ why, you’d be giving him the point of the jaw, and he’d
lay you out stiff with a Dennyhayser !”
# * *
“Honest” John Phelps, who in days gone by
used to act as judge for the Oxford and Cambridge boat-
races, has pegged out at the age of 86. Phelps was only
once called upon to use his intelligence at the winning-
post, and that was in the celebrated year of the “ dead-
heat,” when Oxford palpably won. The story went that
the old man gave his first decision as “ a dead-heat by
two yards,” afterwards sticking to the first part of the
verdict when its original stupidity was pointed out to
him. That was the last appearance of Honest John in
this important capacity.
# * *
The totalisator business cannot long survive
the crushing penalties now imposed in Sydney. Fines
of £IOO and £lO 10s costs, or six mouths in default, are
now ladled out to all caught in the act.
* # ‘ *
Tantallon, the horse that was industriously
reported to be better than Carbine at Melbourne Cup
weights, showed a decent bit of form by winning the
Geelong Gold Cup in a canter.
* * *
Jim Phillips, the Melbourne Cricket Club pro-
fessional, says that, having seen and played against all
the English crack batsmen and bowlers, George Giffen is
not only the best all round cricketer in the world, but is
the best batsman as well.
# * *
At some recent Cobargo (N.S.W.) sports, a prize
of £2O was given by—hold your breath hard —the local
editor!
* * #
A Voice; “Dear Bulletin, —About the Ger-
man clerks who are beginning to swarm also in Austra-
lian mercantile houses. One advantage they have is an
indifference to cricket and football, not regarding play-
games as the be-all and end-all of existence. Whatever
good is a young fellow in business if his head’s full of
football and cricket, while he is cockered up on Saturday
afternoons into thinking himself something big, as a
centre of barrackers, and all the business day there are
chums dropping in on important fixtures, and other
sporting affairs.”

sented Burge and Murphy with an address of congratula-

“Eish ” Smart, the English skater, has won the
mile championship of the Netherlands. Amateur Donog-
hue, of America, won the amateur mile.

William Cook, junr., third of the name in the
billiard world, won a London amateur handicap
recently.

Martin Denny is matched to fight Sam Baxter,
Burge’s victim, in Melbourne for £IOO in March.
* # *
New South Wales won the Intercolonial match
by an innings and 94 runs. Scores, N.S.W. 465 ; Victoria
181 and 190. In Victoria’s second innings Blackham made
50, Houston 24, Walters 20, and Morris 29. Each province
has now won 23 games.
* * *
Griffo’s head is getting decidedly swollen. He
gave a handsome girl, big enough to make four pugs, of
his class, a lift in a buggy over some loose sand at Bondi,
recently. When she got down, he said: “Now you can
go and tell your friends you’ve had a drive with the
champion.”
* * *
At the Manly Regatta the handicap sailing race
was won by Mr. J. Hanson’s Lizzie, 18ft., with 6min.
allowance: the club maiden single sculls by H. F. Booth,
9st. 21bs.; the handicap outrigger race by G. Ruwald,
Manly R. 0., lost. 61bs. (he has now got his tail up
properly and talks about the N.S.W. amateur champion-
ship) from scratch ; and the club skiff race by X. Parkin,
10st. 61bs.
* # *
The Norwood cricket club—Giffen’s team —has
been the champion eleven of Adelaide for many years,
and recently, by defeating Hindmarsh, again virtually
secures the championship.
* * *
Frank Slavin and Charles Mitchell are matched
to box ten rounds for a purse given by the London
National Sporting Club in February. Mitchell’s day is
long past.
* * *
Coburn, the Irish-Yankee fighter, died the
other day. His only notable exploits were evading fights
with Mace, and, on another occasion, fighting a draw with
Jem in a Missouri swamp after 3( hours in the ring. It
was said at the time that Mace didn’t want to win,- now it
is said he daren’t. Harry Poulson, a different sort of
pippin, is also dead. He nearly beat Tom Sayers in ’56,
when they fought for 3hrs. Bmins. for a £IOO stake.
* * *
Jacques Ibbot, the apple-eating athlete and
fasting-man, has been matched to box Pablo Fanque at
Sydney Australian Athletic Club.
* * »
The Sydney yacht Volunteer proved a veritable
Triton among the minnows on Anniversary Day, beating
the SaO, the lolanthe, the Arrow and the Tritonia. It is
a pity that so successful a yacht, and so plucky a sports-
man as the owner, do not meet with competitors from
neighbouring waters. Her latest performance was up to
championship form without doubt.
» # *
At West Maitland races on Saturday last, the
Free Handicap had to be struggled for three times before
a final decision could be given. Alter Petrel had run
home first a protest was laid, on the ground that the
start took place in front of the post, and the objection
being upheld, the horses'were ordered out again. Then
Cupid won, hut another protest proved fatal to him, and
the lot were sent on their third journey. This time
Petrel won again, and well earned the prize.

Salvator, the great American racehorse, is now
in one stable with Darebin and Sir Modred.

It is quite on the cards that Griffq has by no

A stallion called Nelson, with a record of
2min. lOJsecs., is supposed to be the best trotter in the
j world. It is expected that he will some day trot a mile
I in 2min. 6secs.
* # #
Beyond a doubt Kingsmill, the Queensland
j darkie, is now the best short-distance runner in Australia.
I This he proved by the way he lately put down Joe Price
| with a start at Botany. Samuels and M'Garrigal are
j “ off ” and can never return to their old form. Williams
| is also out of form, but the robust “Wally” can always
I be trained back.
# * #
j “Toby ” Moran, an excellent judge of pace and
I no end of a “finisher,” is without much doubt the crack
li ;htweight of the day in N.S. Wales.
# * *
Gough made a fatal mistake by keeping Vespasia
| so far back in the Rosehill Handicap. The pace for the
first six furlongs was miserably slow, and he had no show
of giving Repose a 15-lengths start down the straight.
One of the finest-looking two-year olds of the
| season, Stromboli, full brother to Volcano, made his
| appearance in the Nursery Handicap at Rosehill on
j Saturday last. , He started favourite and should have
) won, but Huxley brought him altogether too late, and
i running a bit green he was beaten by a short neck.
# * #
Victor Hugo ran a great horse with 9st 51b in
the Clyde Handicap. Williams brought him with a good
run, but he shirked it the least bit at the finish, and
suffered defeat by the shortest of heads from Cobar. The
finish was very close ; indeed, a good many, especially the
backers of Hugo, said he had won. In Mr. Olliffe,
however, we have a square judge, and as he was in the
only position to judge with certainty, there cannot be
much doubt on the subject.
* # *
Bushman has at last won a race on N.S. Wales
j soil. In the Steeplechase at Rosehill bis fencing was
| faultless, and, well handled by Paddy Nolan, he made up
I a good deal for the many disappointments he has dealt
j out to the Sydney public.

j about the Bird crowd. ' “Tottie Fewclothes” and “Mr.
| and Mrs. Titefit ” et hoe genus are all stolen from London.

The 100 yards Amateur Swimming Champion-
ship of N.S. Wales, and the gold medal of the Enterprise
Club, will be competed for at Sydney Natntorium on 18th
February. A dual club has been started at these baths
to promote the hydropathic intercourse of the sexes, and
has already a large membership.

unfixed.
