All the red-nosed men in Sydney now say
that their affliction is due, not to grog but to
“ indigestion.” Alcohol is rather hard to digest.
A miner named O’Donnell claims to have
discovered in New Guinea alluvial gold prospects
equal to anything that has ever been found in
Victoria.
General Booth announces that he has
received £100,000, and will soon hold a monster
thanksgiving service. Sounds something like “tup
pence more and up goes the donkey.”
The Universal Review died recently in
full possession of all its senses. In proof, Quilter
devoted its dying breath to the framing of an un-
answerable indictment against Bandit Stanley on
23 counts.
The Superior of the Passionist Fathers
is now on a visit to Australia. From what we
have seen of. the Passionists, the name of their
order must be derived from the fact that they get
into a great temper if the turkey happens to be
overdone.
On the morning of the wedding, Mr.
Andrew Tennant, father of the lady who, the
other day, became the wife of Captain Anstrutber
Thomson, A.D.O. to Governor Kintore, handed
over to the bride’s trustees deeds representing a
gift of £20,000.
It is nearly four years since Mr. C. F.
Stratton threw open to public use the inode!
hotel, built, owned, and occupied by him at
Waverley, Syd., and to-day what was then deemed
a doubtful experiment stands crowned with
triumphant success, lor the house is not only well
patronised by the local residents and visitors from
abroad, but has been made a home by commercial
people, and those retired from business who prefer
the fresh air, fine view, open expanse, quiet and
cool salubrity of Waverley to the heat, noise and
bustle of the great city, of which, however, they
are within easy distance by ’bus, tram or cab.
This handsome and commodious building, which
cost nearly £10,000, is situated at the corner of
Nelson and Cowper Streets, Waverley, and looks,
uninterruptedly, on the vast expanse of the Cen-
tennial Park, a reservoir of ozone and a heritage
of future joy to the people of N.S. Wales, and is
an architecturally handsome and undeniably com-
fortable and commodious building, with verandahs
back and front; it is of four stories, inclusive of the
basement, in height. On the first floor are situate
the public and private bar-rooms, both finished in
Huon pine and cedar, the former arranged in the
London style with sit-down stalls, swinging
doors, &e., and the latter _most elegantly
appointed ; the dining-room of 35 by 22 feet, and
the billiard-room well ventilated and cosy (40 by
32 feet, containing two of Halbert’s best tables)
and numerous parlours. On the second story may
be found the public drawing-rooms and a number
of single and double bedrooms furnished through-
out with great taste, and yet with the utmost
solidity. The floor above is also given over to
sleepmg accommodation, and both floors are
thoroughly equipped with bath-rooms, where hot
and cold water and all the other indispensables
of civilised existence may be always obtained.
Electric-bells are laid on throughout the building.
In the basement, asphalted and cemented from
end to end, are to be found the kitchen, splendidly
appointed, the servants’ hall, several storerooms
the beer and wine cellars, and the servants’ apart
ments. Outside are to be found the stables,
carriage-rooms, laundries, and public offices, while
a public ball adjoins the hotel, in dimension 95
by 45 feet, capable of seating several _ hundred
persons. Stratton’s Hotel, Waverley, is one of
the institutions of that charming suburb.*
IN THE INTERESTS
of
THE GENERAL PUBLIC.
(From the Leeds Saturday Journal.)
It is very seldom that we pay much atttention
to reports circulated in reference to the virtue of a
patent medicine, as we have always considered
that reports pf this nature were more or less
fictitious, and gotten up for the purpose of creat-
ing a demand from the public. Especially have
we taken this view of those medicines largely
advertised to be taken internally, but the publica-
tion in various papers of the wonderful results
obtained from using that famous remedy St>
Jacobs oil, and from the fact that it is a remedy
for outward application only, led us to make trials
of it in our own family. One was a case of lum-
bago of long standing, which had previously
resisted every form of treatment and was per-
manently cured by a few applications of the oil.
Another was a sprained ankle with severe pain.
This was instantly relieved and permanently
cured in a few days by the use of St. Jacobs oil.
While for severe toothache and neuralgia it acted
almost like magic. Our own experience in using
the oil, as above indicated, was so highly satis-
factory that we determined in the interests of
the general public to make a thorough local
investigation among dealers and others who
had sold and used St. Jacobs oil. Recognising
Messrs. Taylor and Co. (Limited), as head-
quarters for all proprietary medicines, we
called at their Briggate store, and, on mak-
ing known the object of our visit to the
general manager, he stated that their three stores
in Leeds were selling more than a thousand
bottles of St. Jacobs oil every week, and the
trade was constantly increasing—that it was the
most popular remedy sold, and was highly spoken
of by every one who used it. The manager said
he had heard hundreds of people say they had
been permanently cured of rheumatism, neuralgia,
sciatica, and similar complaints by the use of
this famous oil, and that although they purchased
the oil in immense quantities direct from the pro-
prietors in London, the demand was so great that
it was with difficulty they were able to keep a
sufficient supply on hand.
THE ENTERPRISING YANKEE.
To produce a watch so simple in construction
that it could be sold cheaper than any other, and
still be a reliable timekeeper, has been the pro-
blem for watchmakers the world over. The Swiss
developed the making of watches by hand to the
highest point, at the same time cheapening hand
labour to the utmost possible extent. The Ameri-
cans sought a solution in a different direction by
making the parts of their watches by machinery,
and interchange. However, the cost of manufac-
ture still remained tco great for the watch of the
million. An enterprising Yankee Company
finally solved the problem by dispensing with the
greater number of parts of a watch (reducing the
number from 1(50 to 57!) makiner all of them by
machinery, and so easy of adjustment that expert
labour was not needed to put them together.
This crowning achievement in the history of
watchmaking was accomplished by the Waterbury
Watch Company in their Waterbury Watch,
which, though cheap, is as perfect in its parts,
and as reliable in its timekeeping, as any watch
made. . .
Series “ E ” —the best form of the original
Waterbury—is a good serviceable watch produced
at a cost within the reach of all. Keyless, re-
liable, durable, and accurate, it is without a peer
for the man or boy in moderate circumstances.
Series “J” (gents’ size) and “L ” (ladies’ size)
are the latest productions of this famous factory.
They are short-winding, stem-winding, enamel-
dialled, jewelled, and dust-proof watches, with
duplex escapements; and are just as good time-
keepers as anyone could wish to carry. They
have been unanimously declared to be perfect
marvels of mechanical ingenuity. All these
watches are made by the best automatic machinery
and thus, in event of accident, can be efficiently
repaired at a nominal cost, at any of the com-
pany’s repair depots or agencies, established for
the convenience of wearers in various centres
throughout the colonies. The genuine Water-
bury Watches may be obtained from any respect-
able dealer or storekeeper. Price, Series “ E,”
13s. (id.; Series “J” or “L” (enamel dial).

Insist upon having Wolfe’s Schnapps.
Someone suggests that when the Laureate-
ship becomes vacant, Queen Victoria should give
the laurels and madeira to Jean Ingelow. Quite
right. Society women are the best grovellers
after all. It is too late in the world’s history for
anything in the shape of a real white man to
debase his genius by slobbering to order over so
miserable a set as the Hanoverian dynasty. The
Laureateship is a post which could now only be
appropriately tilled by a rhyming monthly nurse.
If out of sorts try Wolfe’s Schnapps.

The New Exodus.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“ General Booth has collected £100,000 in aid of his
scheme for exporting paupers.”—Daily Press.
“ Sweep the gutters, cast the garbage on the clean,
unlittered ways,
In the hope its deadly odours may be sweetened
by the breath
Of the Southern air fresh blowing, and the bright
unclouded rays
Of a Southern sun may rob it of the venom of
its death.”
So cry they who made the garbage, they who
make the gutters flow
With the millions marching ever in the caravan
of crime,
With the hordes of pallid famine, with the thralls
of want and woe,
With the souls who reel, sin-sodden, all along our
piteous time.
The flood lias touched patrician feet, has stained
patrician clothes,
Has surged around the lofty peaks where proud
patricians sit ;
The odours of the charnel reek in each patrician
nose,
So arise they, in all charity, and cry “Away
with it.”
And the prelates from their places exclaim “Lo !
we have tried
To save them by the Gospel, by our sermons,
and by prayer;
Six feet deep have we with Bibles covered Eng-
land far and wide ;
We have failed. Great is our failure. Now
suppose we give them air.
“ Suppose we try sea-voyages as cure for chronic
crime,
And evict the ‘social problem ’ from the kennels
where it cowers '!
As the poor ‘ are always with us ’ we can spare
them for a time ;
‘Bear ye one another’s burdens’—let our
colonists bear ours !”
Then the lords of opportunity applauded,
everyone,
From the man who owned a palace to the man
who owned a shed,
From the man who owned a county where the
grouse and partridge run,
To the man who owned a property six hasty
steps might tread.
No! Not so! Australia’s farming must be good
from soil to seed.
Can you free the seed you send us from heredi-
tary taint ?
Shall we choke our comjng harvest with the
poisondom of weed !
For your sinner’s still a sinner, though he's
washed and labelled “ saint.”
Oh, Beloved Mother Country, all the poverty and
crime
That you seek to send, post-paid, across three
thousand leagues of foam,
Are results of your own lust and greed ; so, there-
fore, for this time
Let your charity’s beginning like its stopping
be at home.
Turn the millions on your acres, and evict the
fallow deer,
Force the idler from his carriage, let him earn
his daily bread.
If you will not—keep your vassals till in some dark
harvest year
Your social system’s shatter’d’ ’neath Red
Revolution’s tread.
EDWARD J. DEMPSEY.
One of the most amusing sketches in Lady
Dufferin’s new book is one of the Afghan Ameer’s
private executioner, clad in red velvet, and always
having his axe and strangling-rope fixed to his
person.
According to the cable, even the coolies
loathe the European blacklegs. It is a melancholy
fact, by the way, that during the recent great
Australian strike the blacklegs were chiefly Eng-
lish and Australian-born men; the “foreigners,”
almost to a man, refused to “turn dog.”
Good old Dr. Steel, the successor of John
Dunmore Lang as a Chinese enthusiast, assures
us that “the Chinese are awakening to a proper
understanding of Christianity.” That’s why they
resolutely avoid it. Confucius has believers who
practise what he inculcated ; Christ has represen-
tatives who preach His doctrines and grab wildly
to shake hands with Mammon.
A handsome piece of “skite” from the
Broken Hill (N.S.W.) Budget :
The whole of Pitt-street, Sydney, with the buildings on
either side of it, could lie hidden in payable ore in the
Broken Hill lode, and then there would be room for
Collins-street in Melbourne, Bundle-street in Adelaide,
and all the public-houses and churches in Australia, as
well as the Royal Navy and every merchant steamer in
the world, and supposing all the ore represented by these
streets and buildings and ships were taken out, there
would, to all appearances, still be left in the mines 50
years of profitable work, even if the quantity of ore
treated weekly were twice as great as it is now.
The trial and execution of Mrs. Pearcey
created a tremendous sensation in London. From
the comments of the papers it is plain that the
opposition of the intelligent classes to .the death-
penalty issteadily increasing. Mrs. Pearcey was
executed in face of the facts that she had a dis-
tinct history of four attempts at suicide, a well-
established hereditary predisposition to mental
disorder, and several officially-recorded attacks of
epilepsy. Dr. Forbes Winslow and five other
well-known lunacy experts vainly prayed for her
life. Her strangulation, like the thousands which
preceded it, conveys no moral to the potential
murderer. The British gallows may be immov-
able : but it cannot deal with the impulse which
is irresistible.
Au Auburn (N.S.W.) press-writer died
recently, and his successor in the ink-slinging line
ventures to remonstrate with “ our local preachers
and Sunday-school teachers ” for impressing upon
the minds of the lambs of the flock that the un-
lamented and lamentable deceased is, “ without a
doubt, burning in hell.” A stave —from The
Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin, —During the present week
an exceedingly pleasant “ fill-’em-Up-again ” soft
of dog began to stroll about Maryborough, Q.,
making the acquaintance of members of the com-
munity well-known for the determined stand they
had made against all attempts to collect amounts
alleged to be due by them for a certain biographical
work recently delivered to their order. “ Now,
sir,” he said to a victim who had fully explained
how shamefully he had been swindled by the
canvasser, “lean see you have been ‘had,’and,
so far as you are concerned, please consider that
my mission here is to chew pine-apples and
mangoes and to enjoy the scenery. Nevermind
your account. Before I goif you wish to pay me
you can, but if you do not, don’t, and there’s an
end to it. Still, you will join me in a drink
and be friends. Besides, I am advised from head-
quarters that there must have been some sharp
dealing as they think a man of your reputation
would scorn to repudiate any fair demand.” To
make a long story short, he contrived somehow to
impress each victim with a sense of the moral and
legal obligation he was under to pay the money,
and wound up with expressions of extreme regret
that he would be compelled to appear in court
against him when the time’arrived. “You are
the bull of the ring, you know, and we must beat
you to beat the others.” So much friendship and
respect soon began to tell on the “ bull,” and that,
together with a desire to merit the good opinion
head-quarters had been so kind as to entertain of
him, decided him to owe the money no longer, and
it was paid forthwith. One after another shared
the same fate until the clever party encountered
an undertaker from whom, after trying in vain to
squeeze the full amount, he insisted on receiving
coffins for the balance. The man of mourning,
questioning not the sincerity of the request, agreed,
and to his astonishment the agent hoisted a four-
feet coffin on to his shoulder and walked down the
crowded street to his hotel seemingly indifferent
to the astonishment he created. Accosted by one
of the citizens and asked his intentions he replied,
“ There’s a sick boy down my way. He can’t last
much longer. His mother is pretty poor and I
can let her have this cheap. I have a few more on
hand; (sizing him up) one of polished cedar that
would fit you to a hair without pinching.” Shortly
afterwards he was seen sending his uncanny lug-
gage to the wharf and booking his passage en route
for Sydney.—Yours, Maryborough (Q.)
It has hitherto often been the astonish-
ment of visitors to Sydney that in a semi-tropical
climate, and with unrivalled natural advantages,
people have been so wanting in enterprise as not to
have established long ago that healthful luxury, a
“ Natatorium. ” It is pleasing to note that no
longer can this taunt be applied, for a visit to
400 Pitt-street, Haymarket, will reveal to the
astonished citizen (without exception) the grandest
sea-water artificial swimming baths in the world.
Two enormous basins, each 100 x 50 feet, supplied
with fresh sea-water daily, and renewed as fre-
quently as necessary, afford ample space for lovers
of this manly, invigorating, and healthful art to
disport themselves. The depth of the baths is so
graduated as to suit both swimmers and non-
swimmers ; trapezes and other athletic appli-
ances, suspended from the lofty ceiling, are in
profusion; careful and polite attendants always
at hand to instruct beginners—this in itself
must command the baths to parents. Special
hours are reserved for ladies and children, during
which experienced female instructors are in con-
stant attendance. The dressing-rooms round the
baths are models of comfort and elegance, and
are replete with all the necessaries incidental to
bathing, whilst the splendid ventilation and
absolute cleanliness of the entire establishment
reflect the greatest credit on the management.
The charges of admission are so moderate as to
bring these baths into general use. The greatest
care being taken at the same time to keep them
select. Refreshments in the shape of tea, coffee,
etc., can be had at the entrance, and will bo
found of the best description. Visitors will omit
one of the sights of Sydney if they fail to see
these baths, an illustration of which, with the
splendid hotel attached to them, may be seen in
this issue. The popularity of these baths is already
assured and their success has been quite pheno-
menal.*'
What matter where?
You needn’t care.
You have no cause to holler.
When comes your end
You’ll find your friend ;
Why need you strain at collar ?
You’ll see his phiz,
For where he is,
There you are bound to foller.
Confidence.
Confidence in one’s self goes far towards success
The man who is able to tackle an ugly job, full}
sure that he can devise the ways and means oi
successfully completing the work, is the mar
valuable to an employer, and the one of most
value to the community. All men are not, how
ever, gifted alike in this respect; one man has the
capacity to think the matter out and reason with
himself how he can best overcome the difficulties
before him, while another will be overwhelmed
with the trouble. This latter kind is frequently
met with in this life, men who have not the force
of character to fight their way through difficulties,
nor the energy to carry their undertakings to a
successful issue. They are mostly thin, nervous
creatures, with a slow pulse and a weak heart.
They are physiologically constructed the same as
the robust and persistently energetic men. Such
men should take good exercise and generous food,
abstain from excitement, and where indigestion
and sallow or pale complexion, indicates anaemia
and sluggish liver, a proper course of Clements
Tonic will do much good. It will invigorate the
stomach and bowels, quicken the pulse, and revive
the depleted strength of the heart, aid digestion
and strengthen the nerves and promote the action
of the skin, liver and kidneys. Clements Tonic
has been used with such satisfactory results, that
we have great pleasure in submitting the following
evidence for perusal by our readers. The Rev. R
Laverty, Port Macquarie, N.S.W., writes:—“l
have much pleasure in bearing my testimony to
the efficacy of Clements Tonic. I was promptly
cured of facial neuralgia by the use of Clements
Tonic, experiencing relief in a few hours. It is
the best tonic I know.” Miss Emily Thorman, 95
Wilson-street, Golden Grove, N.S.W., says :—“I
suffered with liver complaint and indigestion for
two years and could neither eat nor sleep and was
reduced to almost a shadow. I tried all the
remedies I could think of. Reading of Clements
Tonic I resolved to try a bottle, and am thankful
to say it has made a new woman of me, and I am
only too thankful to be able to add my grateful
testimony.” H. E. Neale, Esq., Loco. De-
partment, Wellington, N.S.W., writes :—“ My
daughter has for the past seven months been
suffering excruciating agony from Neuralgia and
also extreme nervousness. I obtained for her
several so-called specifics but without avail. Sho
had no appetite and could scarcely eat a particle
of food, and was almost reduced to a skeleton
from want of food and loss of sleep. I saw
Clements Tonic highly spoken of, I determined to
try it and after she had taken six doses the pain
left, and she at once commenced to improve. She-
lias taken two bottles and is now quite herself
again. I shall always recommend Clements!
Tonic.”

Verse We Have Head.
The Barings were near taking up the con-
struction of the Victorian railways, 35 years ago.
Childers, arriving in London from Melbourne, got
a commission to return as the Barings’ agent, but
could not negotiate the Railways, though he
would have succeeded but for O’Shanassy replacing
Haines as Premier. A smart man of the time was
Gabrielli, who provided a million loan for the
Melbourne Corporation, though he hadn’t a
copper.
One of the recent rumours started by the
religious British press is that Parnell attended
the meetings of the Home Rule members with a
loaded revolver in his pocket, and manifested a
badly-disguised inclination to open fire on Hcaly
or any other individual who disagreed with him.
The Bulletin is in a position to contradict this
report on reliable cable authority. Instead of a
revolver Parnell carried a bow and arrow, a
boomerang, one battering-ram, two torpedoes, a
dozen stink-pots, a small portable gallows, and a
quantity of nitro-glycerine, but otherwise he was
unarmed and manifested no warlike tendencies.
Another item of telegraphic information which
has reached this paper states that her Majesty in-
tends to elope with the Pope. Further details
are expected shortly.
Apropos to the present or recent Indian
rising, old Californians tell diverting stories of
how they used to raise a pool of, say, 5000 dollars
whenever a warlike tribe of Injins began to worry
the palefaces. A deputation would then wait
upon the boss chief of another warlike tribe, and
make arrangements to buy 250 scalps of the oppo-
sition lot at 20 dollars per scalp ! At the end of
a fortnight the whole pool would be applied for,
the rising suppressed, and the victorious mer-
cenaries busily engaged in drinking themselves
off the face of the earth. Thus the astute Cali
forniam syndicate decimated two tribes at com-
paratively small expense, and when 'the Govern-
ment at Washington desired to make enquiries
into this “very serious ” matter, they were satisfied
with a sort of unofficial assurance, emanating
from nowhere in particular, that it wasn’t a
serious matter at all. Nowadays they seem to be
wiping out the noble savage on the regular
system, which is more English, vou know, but
very costly and cumbersome. The army, we
grieve to read, has even sunk to the absurdity of
“ capturing prisoners” when civilisation is panting
for scalps.
A pleasant social evening was spent at the
Melbourne quarantine station the other day on
the arrival of the smallpox-stricken Kelton. There
are various highly-paid officials whose business it
is to fool round the quarantine ground, but they
were all absent, and nobody could find them,
The bedsteads were there, but the bedding had
been put away somewhere and forgotten, and
most of the keys had been mislaid, so that nobody
could get in, and the only place to lay a patient
was under a tree. Eighteen policemen had been
telegraphed for to form a “cordon,” but most of
them had got lost in the woods, and were cursing
at large and falling over roots. The linen had all
been packed in the oven, and the cooking utensils
had been standing for ten months in the sink, and
the surgical instruments were mostly in the coal-
box, and the only tooth-brush in the place was on
the roof, having been thrown at a cat early the
previous year. Officials of various degrees of
importance wore arriving in haste and falling over
each other at the gate, and shivering round among
the trees because the key hadn't been found, and
the towels and soap Were lying on a s’tmup beside
the front window; and the Kelton herself was
stranded in the mud right opposite the station,
having missed her way up the harbour, and three
tugs were whistling, and burning blue lights.
Also, there was a heavy sea on, with occasional
showers, and among all this frenzy the Health
Department plunged round in the dark and fell
over everything, and wished that it was dead.
Editor Bulletin. Sir, You enquire
about the release of a young man who got 18
months for forging his mother’s name to a p.n.,
the jury in whose case would have returned a ver-
dict of acquittal but for the persistence of the
Judge (Innes), who pointed out that if the accused
were acquitted the public would say there was in
N.S. Wales one law for the rich and another for
the poor. How far this was interfering with the
sacred right of the jury to independence in the
matter I leave to others to judge. But Ido Want
to know when these 15 or 20 men who have no
friends at court and who have been in gaol for
over 10 years (one for 25, another for 18, and so
on) are likely to have their oases considered ? Or
will they be deferred until the jubilee of the
Prince of Wales as King of England occurs? Why
should their cases be undetermined because one
old man stands in the way of the release of the
oldest in confinement ? Ido not know if there be
any truth in the statement that private pique
is at the bottom of this man’s long imprisonment,
but if it be it is a gross reflection on our
common humanity. Have we no Howard in the
colonial Parliament who will for a time put aside
“ the bridge agin his own door ” and make inquiry
into the position of these wretches who are
going through a living death ? I know of one
man in Darlinghurst now who has done six years
out of 10 given him by a cruel judge for what is
known as “sweating” a horse, an offence for
which he would now get 12 months. By the way,
there is a legend about this case, that the judge
sentenced him to five years “cumulative ” for the
saddle, but on the people in court loudly exclaim-
ing “Too much!” he consented to make it
“concurrent.” — Yours, &c., Lex.
f Sydney Star, which evidently has on', its
staff a Melican man, or a Maorilander (who is
next door to a Melican man), thus discourses on
the death last week, at ’Prisoo, through Bright’s
disease, of King Kalakaua, of Hawaii: —
Kalakaua \vas a fine, healthy old specimen of a monarch.
When “ called to the throne ” he was sitting with his legs
dangling over the wharf fishing with the rest of “ the
boys,” and he wouldn’t leave to receive the congratula-
tions of his loving people till he had gathered up his
hooks and lines, and done his share of the knuckle of cold
pork and bread and cheese and onions. “Besides,” he
remarked, “he knew well the moment he was gone they
would 1 sneak ’ a moki or kawi out of one of the fishing
boats from Monowai—the island opposite—and swear they
had caught it, and so ‘break his record.’” He knew his
mates too well to be had on the king racket. He
was a Kammehama, a descent of the great kings of
Hawaii, who drove the other “push” over the Pali, a
precipice of about 5000 ft. “sheer.” Kalakaua had a few
thousand subjects, who were ruled with all the pomp and
.pageantry of an Eastern Court. He had two Parliaments,
or two Houses of Parliament, consisting of members
without boots or trousers. The Minister for Defence
ruled an army of ten, armed with Queen Bess muskets,
and his Majesty’s navy consisted of a whale-boat and a
condemned schooner of 50 tons. His regiments had no
one of a lesser grade than a colonel, and one out of every
ten of his subjects was a judge. The onlv good thing
about the court is the king’s band. The “nigs” take kindly
to blowing, and there is a band of 50 attached to the
court which would hold its own in London or Vienna.
Honolulu is a delightful place. No one ever seems to
work or go to bed except during the day. The
Hawaiians never took kindly to the missionaries, save aS
articles of diet, and they claimed to be able to dis-
tinguish orthodox churchmen from dissenters by the
taste. Add to this that the women ride straddle-legged,
and are as beautful as Houris of Paradise, that it you
tickle the soil with a hoe it smiles in yams and all sorts
of fruits; that it has literally no winter at all nor any hot
summers ; that its waters are blue as indigo save where
they curl like the finest lace over lovely reefs; that its
vegetation, including the stately cocoanut-tree and
banana-groves, is sans compare, and you have an earthly
paradise—one which it is a shame to disturb with what
is called “civilisation.”
The Present Crisis.
When a deed is done for Freedom, through the
broad earth’s aching breast
Rsns a thrill of joy prophetic, trembling on from
east to west;
And the slave, where’er he cowers, feels the soul
within him climb
To the awful verge of manhood, as the energy
sublime
Of a century bursts full-blossomed on the thorny
stem of Time,
For mankind are ope in spirit, and an instinct
bears along
Round the earth’s electric circle the swift flash of
right or wrong;
Whether conscious or unconscious, yet Humanity’s
vast frame
Through its ocean-sundered fibres feels the gush
of joy or shame;
In the gain or loss of one race all the rest have
equal claim.
Once to every man and nation comes the moment
to decide
In the strife of Truth with Falsehood for the good
or evil side;
Some great cause, God’s new Messiah, offering
each the bloom or blight,
Parts the goats upon the left hand, and the sheep
upon the right,
And the choice goes by for ever ’twixt that dark-
ness and that light.
Then to side with truth is noble, when we share
her wretched crust,
Ere her cause bring fame and profit, and ’tis pro-
sperous to be just;
Then it is the brave man chooses, while the
coward stands aside,
Doubting in his abject spirit, till bis Lord is
crucified,
And the multitude make virtue of the faith they
had denied.
Count me o’er earth’s chosen heroes - they were
souls that stood alone,
While the men they agonised for hurled the con-
tumelious stone,
Stood serene, and down the future saw the golden
beam incline
To the side of perfect justice, mastered by their
faith Divine,
By one man’s plain truth to manhood and to God’s
supreme design.
For humanity sweeps onward ; where to-day the
martyr stands
On the morrow crouches Judas, with the silver in
his hands;
Far in front the cross stands ready, and the
crackling faggots burn,
While the hooting mob of yesterday in silent awe
return
To glean up the scattered ashes into history’s
golden urn.
J. R. LOWELL.
Lincoln’s Melancholy.
His Sympathetic Nature and his Early
Misfortunes.
Those who saw much of Abraham Lincoln
during the latter years of his life were greatly
impressed with the expression of profound melan-
choly his face always wore in repose.
Mr. Lincoln was of a peculiarly sympathetic
and kindly nature. These strong characteristics
influenced, very happily, as it proved, his entire
political career. They would not seem at first
glance to be efficient aids to political success ; but
in the peculiar emergency which Lincoln, in the
providence of God, was called to meet no vessel
of common clay could possibly have become the
“ chosen of the Lord.” Those acquainted with
him from boyhood knew that early griefs tinged
his whole life with sadness. His partner in the
grocery business at Salem was “Uncle” Billy
Green, of Tallula, HI., who used at night, when
the customers were few, to hold the grammar
while Lincoln recited his lessons. It was
to his sympathetic ear that Lincoln told the
story of his love for sweet Ann Rutledge.
And he, in return, offered what comfort he
could when poor Ann died, and Lincoln’s
great heart nearly broke. “After Ann died,”
says “Uncle” Billy, “on stormy nights, when
the wind blew the rain against the roof, Abe
would sit thar in the grocery, his elbows on
his knees, his face in his hands, and the tears
runnin’ through his fingers. I hated to see
him feel bad, an’ I’d say, ‘Abe, don’t cry,’and
he’d look up an’ say, ‘ I can’t help it, Bill, the
rain’s afallin’ on her.’” There are many
who can sympathise with this overpowering
grief, as they think of a lost loved one, when
“the rain’s a-falling on her.” What adds poig-
nancy to the grief sometimes is the thought
that the lost one might have been saved.
Fortunate, indeed, is Colin MTherson, of
Shepparton, Victoria, proprietor of Sheppab-
ton News, who writes under date of 24th
May, 1890:—“Nearly three years since I began
to lose my health. Suffering no pronounced
pains at first, with the exception of a general
languid feeling, I was then not able to satisfy my-
self as to the diagnosis of my trouble, but subse-
quent symytoms proved the presence of disease of
the kidneys and liver. I suffered from impaired
digestion, nausea, loss of appetite, and at times
my pains were so intense that I imagined my
constitution was breaking up. Such was my state
of health when I first began the use of Warner’s
Safe Cure. With the use of a few bottles the
languid feeling began to wear off, my appetite,
which I had completely lost, returned, and my
restoration to health was rapid and complete.
Warner’-s Safe Cure has removed every trace of
kidney disease, and I feel better than ever I did
during the past decade.”

A LADY’S VISIT TO A JAPANESE
THEATRE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It would not do for any lady unacquainted
with the manners and customs of the Japanese
generally to enter a native theatre haphazard
Vvith the intention of witnessing a performance,
rot the chances are; should she elect so to do, she
ivquld light upon a play she could hot comfortably
lyitneSs; . To please a Japanese aUaienbe thb shovV
uiilst be brimful, of liptfcprs; or as it;
“ have plenty of sorrow in it.” I had been resi-
dent some time in Yokohama before I ventured
into a Japanese theatre, though I had long wanted
to do so, and there are two excellent play-houses
in the native town which borders'on the Foreign
Concession. One 1 day it was announced that a
party of clever actors from Kioto and Osaka were
to perform a celebrated;play, “The Forty-seven
Ronins,” founded on a tale of Japanese vendetta.
Having seen the grave of the real, actors in the
tragedy, I was most anxious to go, and we went
that very evening.
* * i
A plhy in Japan often commenced early ih
the morniilg and prdceed§ fbr several days. Grbat;
therefore; ;was hiy gobd little Aniah’s dstonishmbnt
ivheti shCjfqpricbyre did hot intefid sthrtihg fbritne
theatre until 'after early dinner in the evening;
and that we should return shortly after 10 p.m.
Dinner over, we found the jinrickshas waiting.
These carriages are a sort of adult-perambulators
drawn generally by one man in the shafts. In the
case, however, of hilly country or a long journey
two men, tandem, draw while one pushes behind,
# * *
Though the sun had set it wa's not dark.
The sky was beautifully clear and the air fresh
and bracing. Thoroughly did I enjoy the
ever-varying drive along the Bluff, fenced in here
and there by tall, well-kept hedges, at other
places getting pretty glimpses of surrounding
country. Right before us stood Fusiyama, the
beautiful, garbed in a raiment of snow, that
modestly veiled the loveliness of her peerless form
in draperies of misty cloud. It is a good drive
through the Concession to where the theatre, a
large, square building, stands. Arrived at the
door the first thing to be done was to purchase our
tickets. They turned out to be most substantial
—none of your flimsy pieces of card-board or
paper, but good-sized flat boards, which were
afterwards taken by one of the attendants who
showed us to our places.
* * *
. On entering we found the theatre in many
respects similar to our own, but more rudely
finished. There was a stage at one end with a
decorated front brilliant in the richest colour; a
row of boxes all around hung with lanterns, and a
flat floor answering exactly to our pit. This floor
was divided into squares and rented to the audi-
ence, the ticket showing proprietorship being one
of the large boards before-mentioned, A platform
goes right through the audience in the pit and
leads to the stage. It is frequently made use of
by the actors to show the beauty of their costumes,
and we were fortunate enough to secure a box
from which a good view of both platform and
stage could be obtained. The stage-front was
ornamented by a beautifully-worked crimson cur-
tain, which I was told had been embroidered by
all the Geishas or singing-girls in the neighbour-
hood, who take unbounded interest in matters
theatrical. The prompter sat behind a brilliantly-
painted latticed screen oh ofie side of the plat-
fbhxi; aiid the Orchestra; tight at .the back of the
stage;, Cbfasisted of about 50_ihusibiahs; iyhb were
seated on a sort of raised dais in rows bnh abovh
the other, their instruments being of the guitar
order. The squares in the pit were occupied
generally by family-parties—grandfather, grand-
mother, son and daughter, and all the olive
branches down to the smallest baby. A visit to
the theatre vvas evidently a great occasion. The
gifls had taken great pains With their drfeSs, theif
phir ifibst .elabofatieiy arraiiged, all their best
hair-pins had been bh. and. a batid q’f bright
crepe peeped out from among the glossy dresses.
» , # *
One family party was seated immediately
in front of us. A friend, who had taken his
water-colours to make a sketch of the inside of
the theatre, touched one of the young girls on the
neck with his brush. Instead of tossing her head,
resenting it as a liberty or looking as if some deep
Insult had been offered, as most of our own girls
ivoUld have dohe, she turned towards us with a
broad stnile , which deepened into a merry laugh
when told in her bwfa language that it was a
big fly.” She was really Very pretty* her face a
perfect tWal lighted tip by. a pair df almond-shaped
black eyes, eyebrows dblicately atqhed, lohg
Straight hose ahd a rosy little inoUth Whibh was a
perfect Gupid’s bow—-disclosing, when she smiled;
two rows of pearly white teeth. Tier face and
neck were liberally decorated with rice powder,
and the creamy white of her skin was accentuated
and brought out by the artificial tingeing of the
lips and cheeks. She had on what was evidently
her best dark-blue silk dress, her scarlet chemise
peeped out from beneath her black velvet collar,
and her dress was bound round her waist by a
broad bright obi or sash made also of silk and
lined with satin. A woman in Japan never does
' her own hair and seldom ties her own sash, this
task nearly always devolving on mother, sister, or
friend, so much depends on the perfect sit of the
bow or fold behind.
j, * * #
Nothing can possibly be more charming
than the manner of a well-brought-up Japanese
woman. It is invariably simple, gentle, and
courteous; there is perfect ease and an utter ab-
sence of self-consciousness; nor is there the least
approach to anything that would strike you as
being forward or wanton. Spartan-like, the
Japanese pay great respect to the aged. All
children are early taught that on them will
devolve the duty of providing for their parents in
old age, guarding them from want and misery of
every kind, and, if possible, maintaining them in
ease and comfort during their last years. We
noticed that the place of honour immediately in
front of each square was given to the old lady and
gentleman of the party. While we were watch-
ing, an attendant came and collected all the san-
dals ; the Japanese having no hats to take off,
remove their shoes instead. These were num-
bered and corresponding numbers given to the
owners. It was a most orderly, well-behaved
company, amongst whom the greatest good-humor
and politeness reigned.
* * *
The neighbourhood of the theatre abounds
in tea-houses, from which you can procure refresh-
ment if you do not wish to leave until the day’s
performance is over. The poorer class of people
go provided, and between the acts little boxes
containing all sorts of refreshments are produced
from unknown quarters : kettles of hot water are
brought iff for the tea-drinkers, and small fur-
hahep galled “habichs,” containing live coals, are
Handed rofifia hi the 'smokers; Meil and WOhleU
alike indulge in tbbiihfeb’. Thfe hijx; they, tiSe is
very small, the bowl being capable of holding thb
tiniest pinch of the weed. Vendors of roasted
pea-nuts take the place of the men who sell
“apples, oranges, and lemonade” in our own
theatres.
# * *
Eveii though you do dot understand the
laflgdagfe; it is quite pbssible tb foilfitV the play
fairly, for theat.tibg is ixlbst natural., The women’s
parts are all taken hy tried,' f '6t though wortieil
appear on the stage in Japan as singers add
dancers, they never take part in a dramatic per-
formance, and the stage make-up is so perfect that
it is hard to realise they are mot really women.
A dramatic scene between Daimio, one, of The
old Feudal lords, and an old retainer was especially
interesting. He entered; with a number of his
retainers; Samurai, in their military dress. The
dignity of his manner, free from affectation of any
kind, and the respect paid to him, devoid of all
obsequiousness, Were extremely pleasing. You
had a gfehtlemaU before you who was capable of
treating his retaiheta With perfect geniality and
kindness, yet without the slightest familiarity.
# ■ * #
The, Saipurai, if, hot all, ‘‘ hobie,” .ivferh
members of old families. 'They forjpefJ, top
military element of Japan and were distinct from
all other classes; they were at the immediate
command of their lord, and wore his crest upon
their dress (a dress especially their own) and bore
two swords— one thrust through the folds of their
belts, another at their side. The wearing of
these swords was a special badge of aristocracy,
and, no matter how poor they might be, gave
them immense privileges over ordinary people.
The under-garment of the Samurais was a sort of
long, loose, kilted trouser, which fell in straight
folds from the waist—in fact, a divided skirt.
They never engaged in trade —which was regarded
as derogatory to the dignity of a soldier. They
received pay in kind, had quarters in the castle
grounds, and were provided in dress and accoutre-
ments by their Daimio, to whom they were ex-
ceedingly loyal. The old feudal castles are still
to be seen, with their stone battlements sur-
rounded by moats covered with lotus-flowers and
water-lilies and approached by drawbridges. The
feudal system, however,, passed away with the old
form of government and the Tycoon. Each
Daimio had his. small court, his ministers and
officials being taken from the Samurai.
# # #
It is related that after the advent of
foreigners a Daimio witnessed a review of British
soldiers in Yokohama. When the exhibition was
over he turned to Sir Rutherford Alcock, who was
at that time British Ambassador, and said :
“ My retinue are few, and their tactics not
worthy of notice after what I have seen; still,”
added he with pride, “ there is not a
man amongst them who, if I say ‘ die!’
will not sacrifice his life at my com-
mand.” And it was quite true. The
retainers were very jealous of any disrespect
shown to their lord, and would immediately
avenge a proffered insult. In this way some very
tragic events have taken place. A Daimio had
but to command one of his subjects to commit
suicide and he was bound in all honour to do so.
The small sword that a Samurai carried In bis
belt was placed there and used for this social
purpose. Under the old Government a man might
do the darkest deeds of violence provided he com-
mitted hari-kari afterwards. This was a very
ttay fdr the Tycboh td settle differences with
his nobles. Both parties bribe Cornfottstbly t»Ut bf
the way there was no further ttofible for him.
for them. But there was left, howevef, the
Vendetta, which was sometimes handed down for
several generations, and on such a plot as this was
founded the drama we had come to witness.

Instead of the scenery slicing jbacky as bttr#
does, there was moveable machinery dn pC&tm
that turned it round and altered it. Boys,with
black caps drawn over their faces, after the!

supposed, like them, to be invisible, followed the

of which candles were fixed. During certain pas-
sages in the play they knelt behind them and
adroitly threw the light upon their faces, so that
the audience might see the different expressions
of countenance. They wept in silence during
the love-passages and partings—there was no
kissing, i The old Japanese (who neither kissed
nor “ cussed ”) knew nothing of this Western
mode of testifying affection. A, little comedy was
occasionally introduced, and the jokes made by
the actors were thoroughly appreciated by the
audience, who laughed, applauded, and called
out the : names of their favourite players, who,
sometimes would good-naturedly answer them
back. The chief actor in Japan is Danjero—he is
their Irving or Booth. ;

Though the heat and the noise of their
drums was a little overpowering, we left the
theatre surprised and delighted at the novelty
and good humour of the entertainment.

“Major Wissman, chief of the German
expedition in East Africa, has been declared in-
sane, and recalled. ” —( Cable.)
Suggestively writes Madame Severine :
Some of the “advanced” women want no defenders-
They wish to fight themselves and suppress man in
duelling, as they would suppress him in political and
conjugal life. Poor man ! I never looked upon him in
this light. I always supposed that he had a role in the
family, in life ; that he owed assistance and protection to
woman, from whom he asked in exchange only partial
obedience. lam too much of a woman to have more than
feminine bravery. I could do my duty before a danger
that threatens the weak, the suffering, and the wounded,
but 1 have only civil, not military, courage. My
“ inferiority ” troubles me not in the least. I like to
be loved and protected, but I can repay' all the love and
protection by affection, mending linen, and preparing
good food. These good women would be grotesque on
the field of honour, and what man would follow them ?
If he wound a woman he is odious ; if he be wounded, he
is ridiculous. Prove the contrary if you can !
How is it that none of the great Frenchwomen,
or, in fact, any of the Frenchwomen at all, howl
for the franchise ?
London Figaro, starting off with the in-
genuous truth that “Lord and Lady Carington
are almost worshipped in Australia,” goes on to
relate a pathetic incident of the “farewell cere-
mony ” —presumably in Sydney—which came
under the notice of some vague, mysterious
“ Spectator,” and was first published to the
world in certain Australian papers of dubious
identity. This spectator (sex unknown) with the
“volleys of cheers still ringing in my ears ” was
startled by the apparition of “a neatly-dressed
girl of some 17 or 18 years, holding the hand! of a
little child of about 12, who was sobbing bitterly.”
Considering that the whole continent was sup-
posed to be weeping for the worshipped departers
at the moment, there was nothing special about
this particular infant’s burst of woe, yet
Spectator “enquired whether the little girl had
been hurt.” Whereupon the young lady ex-
plained that her sister was not exactly hurt from
the exterior, but suffering from a broken heart
because Lady Carington was going away. This
statement aroused the curiosity of Spectator,
whose questions elicited the fact that the little
girl had been laid up in the hospital
and visited more than once by the Governor’s wife.
It was a distressing scene. 'Spectator tried hard
to comfort the little girl, but without avail. “ She
kept repeating in heart-rending accents, “I’ll
never see my guardian angel no more.’” And
there the yarn ends. The “ loyal ” London shop-
keepers and other members of the middle toady
classes who get their ideas of Australian senti-
ment from papers like Figaro would doubtless
like to hear what became of that little girl
after her guardian angel departed. The Bul-
letin will tell them. The child slowly pined
and died until her brain gave way. Then
she arose from her bed in the middle of the night
when the sun was shining (the Australian sun
being high in the Heavens at such times) and went
forth and drowned herself. After which she re-
fused food for several days and eventually ex-
pired, turning her fresh chubby little face to the
wall and saying, “ She wos werry good to me, she
wos.” Just at that moment it occurred to the
bereaved mother to whisper, “Lady Jarsey will
be coming over soon ” in her deceased darling’s
deaf ear. The result can be easily imagined. A
little shudder shook the lifeless form showing that
the departed spirit had returned to its earthly
casket, which was instantly restored to rude
health.

The Earl of Harrowby—whoever he may
be—writes to an English paper stating that ‘ the
Church of England never suggested Free Educa-
tion, and from an educational point of view the
Church is very dubious as to the working of such
a scheme.” The Bishops of Lichfield, Carlisle,
and Rochester jointly denounce Free Education
as “ a worthless and shameless piece of political
expediency,” and the Rev. Lee, of All Saints,
Lambeth, has sent a written snort to the London
Council, in which he curses at the whole school
system because it benefits “ the selfish Radical,
the pampered loafer, and the man in the gutter.”
Poor, corpulent, bloated old hypocrites ! What a
racket there will be some day when the man in the
gutter gets up and squares accounts with them.
Where does the glorious principle of
“ freedom of contract ” begin? A few weeks ago
tenders were called for the erection of a residence
in Ballarat, and the contractor who got the work
sublet the carpentery, plumbing, and painting.
One smart young carpenter, finding the thraldom
of the employer too much for him, sent in a
tender for the woodwork—and got it; whereupon
the successful tenders for the other two branches
threatened to “ throw it up ” if he was allowed to
go on with his contract, as he was not a member
of the Contractors’ Association. And he had to
do it, too, as they would not allow him even the
alternative of joining the association !
Lockwood, the most eminent English
barrister in matrimonial cases, has been urging
that breach of promise actions ought to be
abolished, save where the woman has made some
pecuniary outlay, to be reimbursed.
John Dennistoun Wood has contributed
to the journal of the Koyal Colonial Institute
some lines entitled “The Briton’s Land : a lay of
Imperial unity,” the tenor of which is that
Britain has a right to own the whole earth.

While struggling to make headway, George
Sand called for encouragement on M. Keratry, a
popular, middle-aged novelist. Found him with
his young wife, who took no sort of notice of
George Sand, and Keratry’s advice was: “Try
rather to produce children than novels,” where-
upon Madame Dudevant retorted ; “I think yon
had better take that advice yourself.”
Governor “ Jarsey ” admits that he has
a weakness for mining speculations. Well, he has
struck the right spot to humour his taste. Aus-
tralia holds tlie richest gold, silver, and tin mines
in the world —viz., the Mount Morgan (Q.), the
Broken Hill Proprietary (N.S.W.), and the Mount
Bisohoff, Tasmania. It also holds the meanest
lot of wild-cat concerns on which lord or slave
ever “ went broke. ”
The Census Fiend is due round about on
5th April, and a sinking feeling occurs in the
Gushing Thing who is never a day over 18 on her
birthday, and a. strong, inward foreboding per-
vades the innermost part of the gentleman with
dyed hair, and life is beginning to mock dismally
at the gold-washed widow who wants to keep her
ten ravenous olive-branches strictly on the weather
bow, so to speak. By the bye, all the domestic
animals have to be billed in due form, and the
blue pencil will be busy in at least one Sydney
house —where they have 15 full-grown Thomas
felines to have their age, ailments, and religious
convictions deeply gone into, and one of the pam-
pered darlings has a name six syllables long.
The Empress Eugenie, it is recorded, once
fell a victim to the vulgar desire for display.
There was to be a grand ball at the Tuileries,
and it entered the oriflamme head of Napoleon’s
consort to make herself even more resplendent
than was her wont. To attain this result, she
gave orders that a magnificent article of jewellery
should be made for her, whose like had never been
seen before. With lavish band the purest dia-
monds were collected, and in order to swell the
number still further, Eugenie did not hesitate to
enter the Treasury and make an inroad upon the
historic jewels of former sovereigns. Among the
rest the ear-rings of the unfortunate Marie An-
toinette were taken, and their gems reft from
their settings, which latter were debased to the
ignominy of “ old gold.” The eventful night
came, and when the ball was at its height, the
Empress entered. All eyes were at once turned
toward her. She was adorned with the new
article of jewellery regarding which all Paris had
been agog with curiosity. It was a diamond
zone, several inches wide, and so brilliant its
dazzling lustre that the Empress seemed cut in
two at the waist-line, and as the gems reflected
the rays from a thousand points of light, the
divided figure walked through the throng of
spectators, who were fascinated and repelled by
the weird effect of two halves of a human form
moving, yet separated from each other, by the
band of light. Eugeni® never wore the diamond
zone again.

Explained his temper “ scotty ; ”

Who slowly drive themselves insane
With slaughtering of niggers.

„ T . 10th December, 1888.
To H. P. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, hut I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum 1 agreed
went under your treatment mAlai-ch last. At th»
lend of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. 1 roiii that time I commenced to improve and
was gaming in weight every day. About a mouth ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty's
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
